ChatGPT said:
Yes ‚Äî that‚Äôs actually a really smart model, and there are a few strong variations on that same ‚ÄúAI + lead generation / matching‚Äù idea that you can run from home with minimal setup.

Your son‚Äôs concept ‚Äî AI data scraping and lead matching ‚Äî sits at the intersection of automation, outreach, and niche targeting. It‚Äôs scalable, high-margin, and can quickly become semi-passive once the data pipelines are running. You can definitely do something similar, even in a different niche or with a unique spin.

Below are 5 business models in the same vein, with examples, required tools, setup cost, and how they make money.

1. AI-Powered Niche Lead Generation Agency
Model: Scrape or source public data (using AI + no-code tools) to build hyper-targeted lead lists for specific industries ‚Äî then sell those lists or charge clients monthly for new qualified leads.

Example niches:

Wedding suppliers ‚Üí matched with engaged couples

Local trades (electricians, gardeners) ‚Üí matched with property developers

Freelancers ‚Üí matched with small businesses needing short-term help

Sustainable brands ‚Üí matched with ethical stockists or influencers

How to do it:

Use tools like Phantombuster, Clay.com, Apollo.io, Browse.ai, or Bardeen.ai to scrape or enrich leads.

Use ChatGPT or Claude to clean, qualify, and segment leads (e.g. filter by location, job type, company size).

Deliver leads via Google Sheets, Airtable, or automated emails.

Monetisation:

Charge ¬£200‚Äì¬£1,000/month per client for fresh verified leads.

Sell one-off lead lists on Fiverr, Gumroad, or directly to agencies.

Startup cost: <¬£100/month for automation tools.
Scalability: High (automate 80% of the work).
Speed to income: 2‚Äì4 weeks if you start with a narrow niche.

2. AI ‚ÄúJob Connector‚Äù Platform (Micro B2B Matching)
Model: Like your son‚Äôs idea ‚Äî create a simple matchmaking service between clients and contractors (or buyers and sellers), powered by AI search and automation.

Variations:

Match local creatives (photographers, designers, etc.) with small business projects.

Match influencers with small brands seeking collaborations.

Match event venues with suppliers (caterers, decor, entertainment).

How to do it:

Use Airtable + Softr / Glide or Notion + Zapier to build a searchable directory.

Scrape opportunities and profiles (via LinkedIn, Upwork, job boards, or Instagram) and let AI match them.

Send automated introductions via email or form submissions.

Monetisation:

Subscription for access (¬£29‚Äì¬£99/mo)

Commission on successful matches (5‚Äì10%)

Featured listings or ads.

Startup cost: <¬£50 if you use no-code builders.
Scalability: Very high ‚Äî can evolve into a proper SaaS.
Speed to income: 1‚Äì2 months if you build a small MVP and start onboarding users manually.

3. AI Research & Prospecting Service for Agencies
Model: Offer done-for-you outreach data to marketing, PR, or recruitment agencies. They always need qualified contacts ‚Äî you can automate the research side.

Example:
An agency wants 100 marketing directors at UK skincare brands ‚Üí you automate:

Scraping LinkedIn or websites.

Enriching data (emails, socials, company size).

Using AI to verify relevance and write short custom intro lines for outreach.

How to do it:

Use Phantombuster + Clay + GPT-4 + Google Sheets.

Automate data collection and copywriting for cold outreach.

Monetisation:
Charge per lead batch (e.g. ¬£100 for 200 contacts) or offer subscription research packages.

Startup cost: ~¬£50‚Äì¬£100/month for automation tools.
Scalability: Excellent ‚Äî easy to outsource manual QA.
Speed to income: Within 3 weeks once you offer it to local agencies.

4. AI-Fuelled ‚ÄúTender Alerts‚Äù or ‚ÄúProject Watch‚Äù Service
Model: Aggregate new job or tender opportunities from multiple sources, summarize with AI, and send alerts to subscribers.

Examples:

‚ÄúCreative Brief Alerts‚Äù for designers, filmmakers, photographers.

‚ÄúLocal Council Contract Watch‚Äù for small trades or maintenance firms.

‚ÄúStartup Funding Watch‚Äù summarizing open grants and startup competitions.

How to do it:

Scrape or RSS-monitor relevant sites daily (Tenderstream, local councils, Upwork, etc.).

Have ChatGPT or Claude summarise opportunities and categorize them.

Send daily or weekly email digests automatically via Beehiiv or Substack.

Monetisation:

Monthly subscription (e.g. ¬£15‚Äì¬£30/mo).

Sponsorships once you have niche audience.

Startup cost: ¬£0‚Äì¬£30 (Beehiiv + scraping tools).
Scalability: Very high ‚Äî easy to expand categories.
Speed to income: 3‚Äì4 weeks once you build an email list.

5. AI-Driven Data Subscription Business
Model: Curate and sell fresh, valuable data in any niche where information changes fast ‚Äî events, new businesses, new properties listed, etc.

Examples:

‚ÄúNew Venues & Openings‚Äù list for event planners.

‚ÄúRecently Funded Startups‚Äù for recruiters and B2B sales.

‚ÄúInfluencers in Emerging Niches‚Äù list for PR agencies.

How to do it:

Use Google Alerts, Apollo.io, and web scraping to collect updates.

Use ChatGPT to summarize, categorize, and clean data weekly.

Sell access via Gumroad, Notion, or a paid newsletter.

Monetisation:

One-off list sales or recurring access subscription.

Startup cost: Minimal (<¬£50/mo).
Scalability: High ‚Äî can run as a solopreneur.
Speed to income: 2‚Äì3 weeks with a solid niche.

üóûÔ∏è 1. Creative Projects Digest
üéØ Goal:

‚öñÔ∏è Option 1 ‚Äî Use the Microsoft 365 email you already have (simplest)
If you‚Äôre happy with using Outlook (instead of Gmail), this is the easiest and cheapest option because it‚Äôs already bundled with your domain.

‚úÖ You‚Äôll get:
A professional address like hello@lumo22.com

Outlook web and desktop access

Office apps online (Word, Excel, etc.)

ü™ú Setup steps:
Log in to your GoDaddy account ‚Üí My Products.

Find your domain (lumo22.com) ‚Üí click Email & Office ‚Üí Set up.

Choose your email username (e.g. hello@lumo22.com) and complete setup.

You‚Äôll get login details for Outlook (via outlook.office.com).

üü¢ Recommended if:
You don‚Äôt mind Outlook and want to save money / keep it simple.

‚öôÔ∏è Option 2 ‚Äî Switch to Google Workspace (Gmail + full Google tools)
If you prefer Gmail, Google Calendar, and Drive integration, you can use your domain on Google Workspace instead of Microsoft 365.
You‚Äôll pay around ¬£5‚Äì¬£10/month per user.

ü™ú Setup steps:
Go to Google Workspace Setup.

Sign up with your domain (lumo22.com).

Choose your plan (the ‚ÄúBusiness Starter‚Äù plan is fine).

Google will ask you to verify your domain ‚Äî it gives you a TXT record to add to your GoDaddy DNS settings:

In GoDaddy ‚Üí Domain ‚Üí DNS ‚Üí Add new TXT record.

Paste in the Google verification code ‚Üí Save.

Google will confirm within a few minutes.

Once verified, Google gives you MX records to replace your Microsoft ones (so email goes to Gmail, not Outlook).

Add those MX records in GoDaddy ‚Üí Save ‚Üí Wait for propagation (~1‚Äì4 hours).

You‚Äôll then be able to log in to Gmail and send/receive from hello@lumo22.com.

üü° Recommended if:
You prefer Gmail‚Äôs interface, collaboration tools, or plan to build a small team (since Google Workspace scales well).

üß† Pro Tip
You can actually start with the Microsoft email (since it‚Äôs free with your domain) and later switch to Google Workspace easily when you‚Äôre ready to scale or bring on others. You‚Äôll just update your MX records in GoDaddy when that time comes.

how do I cancel outlook and switch?
ChatGPT said:
Good question ‚Äî here‚Äôs exactly how to cancel Microsoft 365 (Outlook) from GoDaddy and switch to Google Workspace (Gmail for Business) for your domain.

üß© Step 1: Understand Your Current Setup
Since you got Microsoft 365 through GoDaddy, your email is managed directly in your GoDaddy account under their Email & Office Dashboard (not via Microsoft directly).

That means you‚Äôll need to cancel via GoDaddy, not Microsoft.

üßπ Step 2: Cancel Microsoft 365 on GoDaddy
Go to GoDaddy.com and sign in.

Click your name in the top right ‚Üí My Products.

Scroll to Email & Office.

Find your Microsoft 365 subscription.

Click the ‚ãØ (three dots) ‚Üí Cancel Renewal (or Manage ‚Üí Cancel Subscription, depending on your screen).

Confirm cancellation.

Your mailbox will stop working once the plan expires.

Make sure you back up all emails first if you‚Äôve been using it.

üì• Step 3: Backup Your Emails (if needed)
If you‚Äôve already used Outlook, you can export your emails:

Open Outlook app ‚Üí File > Open & Export > Import/Export > Export to a file > Outlook Data File (.pst).
Save that file somewhere safe ‚Äî you can import it into Gmail later if you want.

üí° Step 4: Set Up Google Workspace for Your Domain
Go to workspace.google.com.

Click Get Started.

Enter your business name, domain (e.g. lumo22.com), and create your first admin account (like sophie@lumo22.com).

Follow the steps ‚Äî Google will guide you to verify your domain ownership.

üìú Step 5: Verify Your Domain in GoDaddy
During setup, Google will show you a TXT record to add to your domain‚Äôs DNS.

Go to GoDaddy ‚Üí My Products ‚Üí Domains ‚Üí DNS.

Under Records, click Add Record ‚Üí Type: TXT.

Paste the record Google gives you.

Save.
Google will detect it within a few minutes to a few hours.

‚úâÔ∏è Step 6: Point Your Email to Google
Once verified, change your MX records to Google‚Äôs:

Priority | Name | Value
1 | @ | ASPMX.L.GOOGLE.COM
5 | @ | ALT1.ASPMX.L.GOOGLE.COM
5 | @ | ALT2.ASPMX.L.GOOGLE.COM
10 | @ | ALT3.ASPMX.L.GOOGLE.COM
10 | @ | ALT4.ASPMX.L.GOOGLE.COM
Delete any existing Microsoft 365 MX records.

Save ‚Äî then within a few hours, your domain‚Äôs email will start routing through Gmail.

‚úÖ Step 7: Log In and Test
Go to mail.google.com and sign in with your new business email (e.g. sophie@lumo22.com).
Send a test message to and from a personal email to confirm it‚Äôs working.

You‚Äôre using the domain with another managed Google account that you own. If so, remove the domain from the other account.

ChatGPT said:
Ah yes ‚Äî that‚Äôs a common and fixable hiccup when setting up Google Workspace on a domain you just bought through GoDaddy üëç

Here‚Äôs exactly what‚Äôs happening and what to do step by step:

‚öôÔ∏è Why It Happens
Google is detecting that your domain (lumo22.com) is already linked to some Google account ‚Äî often because:

GoDaddy automatically created a temporary Google Workspace ‚Äústub‚Äù account during checkout, even if you didn‚Äôt finish setup.

Or a verification record was half-added behind the scenes.

So Google thinks your domain is already ‚Äúowned‚Äù by another Workspace admin ‚Äî even though that‚Äôs you.

‚úÖ Step-by-Step Fix
Option 1: Recover the Existing Admin Account (fastest if possible)
Go to https://admin.google.com

Try signing in with:

admin@lumo22.com

or whatever email you might have entered during domain purchase.

Click ‚ÄúForgot email?‚Äù or ‚ÄúForgot password?‚Äù if you‚Äôre not sure.

Google will send a recovery link to the backup email you used when you bought the domain (your personal Gmail, for instance).

Once you regain access, you can delete the Workspace from inside that admin panel:

Admin Console ‚Üí Billing ‚Üí Subscriptions ‚Üí Cancel Workspace.
After that, you can re-sign up cleanly (it can take up to 1 hour to release the domain).

Option 2: If You Never Created That Account
Go to Google‚Äôs Domain Ownership Form:
üëâ https://support.google.com/a/contact/domain_contact

Fill it out like this:

‚ÄúI recently purchased this domain and I am the new owner.‚Äù

Provide your domain: lumo22.com

Provide your contact email (your personal Gmail works fine).

Google will ask you to verify ownership ‚Äî they‚Äôll send instructions within 48 hours.
They usually release the domain within a day.

Option 3 (for quick check):
Before doing the form, try visiting:
üëâ https://admin.google.com/lumo22.com/VerifyAdminAccount
It may tell you which Google account it‚Äôs associated with ‚Äî sometimes it‚Äôs a hidden stub from GoDaddy.

üí° In the meantime
While waiting, you can still:

Set up your Google Drive and other files using your personal Gmail (we‚Äôll transfer them later).

Start designing your landing page content or supplier signup form.

‚öôÔ∏è The Business Model: Lead Generation / Client Matching
üîπ Core Concept
You act as a middle layer between demand and supply ‚Äî connecting clients (people looking for a service) with providers (the professionals or businesses that deliver it).
You don‚Äôt deliver the service yourself; you own the platform, control the data, and monetise the leads.

üí∞ 1. Revenue Models (Options)
You can combine a few of these depending on your niche:

A. Pay-Per-Lead (PPL)
Businesses pay you a fee per qualified lead you send.

Example: ¬£20‚Äì¬£80 per lead depending on niche.

You track leads via unique links, forms, or call tracking.

Best for: trades, professional services, creative or wellness industries.
Pros: predictable, scalable.
Cons: requires consistent lead flow and good tracking.

B. Commission or Revenue Share
You take a % of closed sales your leads generate.

Example: 10‚Äì20% commission on successful conversions.

Works well if you have strong provider relationships and trust.

Pros: potentially higher payouts per client.
Cons: harder to track; slower payments.

C. Subscription / Listing Model
Businesses pay a fixed monthly fee to be listed or receive matched leads.

Example: ¬£49‚Äì¬£299/month depending on exposure and niche.

Often combined with ‚Äúverified‚Äù or ‚Äúpremium‚Äù listing options.

Pros: recurring revenue, scalable with automation.
Cons: harder to sell until you show value/traffic.

D. Hybrid Model (recommended)
Start with Pay-Per-Lead to validate demand.
Once you have traction and proven ROI, transition best performers to a subscription for recurring revenue.

ChatGPT said:
Excellent ‚Äî that‚Äôs the powerful part of your model, Sophie ‚Äî the data scraping and enrichment is what gives your business a competitive edge and allows you to scale faster than a manual lead gen agency.

Let‚Äôs unpack that clearly, because it‚Äôs a critical layer in your system ‚Äî and it changes how you monetise, automate, and grow.

‚öôÔ∏è DATA-SCRAPING-BASED LEAD GEN MODEL
Instead of waiting for leads to come in through ads or inbound forms, you proactively collect and enrich data on both sides of your marketplace (the clients and the suppliers).

You then use AI to match, score, and deliver high-intent leads.

üîπ 1. What You‚Äôre Actually Building
Think of it as an automated prospect engine sitting under your brand (e.g. Lumo22).
Here‚Äôs how it works structurally:

Scrape or acquire data from public sources ‚Äî directories, event sites, job boards, LinkedIn, Google Maps, social media.

Enrich that data with AI ‚Äî add missing fields (email, website, service type, location, etc.).

Classify and score ‚Äî use an LLM or simple algorithm to predict ‚Äúfit‚Äù between supplier and buyer.

Match and deliver ‚Äî notify suppliers of relevant jobs or opportunities they can bid on.

Monetise by charging per match, per qualified lead, or via subscription access.

üîπ 2. Legal and Ethical Framework
You can do data scraping legally and responsibly if you follow these rules:

‚úÖ Use publicly available data (e.g. company names, websites, contact forms, event listings).

‚úÖ Comply with GDPR: only store business contact data (not personal emails).

‚úÖ Include an opt-out mechanism for any outreach.

‚úÖ Use data for B2B marketing or matchmaking ‚Äî not resale.

Tip: When in doubt, focus on business-to-business data ‚Äî it‚Äôs lawful to use for ‚Äúlegitimate interest‚Äù under GDPR.

üîπ 3. How to Scrape & Enrich Data (No-Code to Low-Code Stack)
Stage	Tool	Function
Scrape	PhantomBuster, Apify, or BrightData	Collect business data from web pages, directories, LinkedIn, etc.
Clean	Google Sheets / Airtable	Filter duplicates, remove junk data
Enrich	Clearbit, Hunter.io, Dropcontact, or GPT API	Add missing info (emails, websites, descriptions)
Match	OpenAI GPT-4 or 5, Claude, or custom Python script	Analyse text and match opportunities
Deliver	Zapier, Make, or custom API	Send matches to clients automatically via email or dashboard
üîπ 4. Monetisation Pathways from Scraped Data
Strategy	Description	Example Price Point
Sell Leads Directly	Sell pre-qualified business leads to service providers	¬£20‚Äì¬£80/lead
Offer Data Access Subscription	Providers pay to view and bid on open leads	¬£49‚Äì¬£299/mo
Create Private Datasets	Build niche datasets (e.g. ‚ÄúTop 500 Wedding Venues UK‚Äù) and sell access	¬£199‚Äì¬£499 per dataset
AI Matching Portal	Use your scraped + enriched data to run automated matchmaking (like ‚ÄúTinder for gigs‚Äù)	¬£5‚Äì¬£20 per match
üîπ 5. Scaling with AI Matching
Once your dataset is growing, you can train or prompt an AI model to match incoming client requests to supplier profiles.

Example workflow:

Client posts ‚ÄúNeed a lighting designer for event in Manchester.‚Äù

AI looks through your database ‚Üí filters suppliers by:

Category: Lighting / Events

Location radius

Availability (based on scraped data)

It sends out automated ‚Äúbid invitations‚Äù to top 5 matches.

Each supplier pays for:

Access to bid (¬£5‚Äì¬£10), or

Monthly membership for unlimited matches.

That‚Äôs how you create recurring, passive revenue on top of your dataset.

üîπ 6. Realistic Income Projection with Data Scraping

You said:
How can I imitate the COS font in canva
ChatGPT said:
Excellent question ‚Äî and you‚Äôre absolutely right to look to COS for inspiration. Their branding is timeless, minimal, and quietly luxurious ‚Äî exactly the kind of understated confidence that would elevate Lumo22.

Here‚Äôs how to replicate the COS aesthetic in Canva, step by step üëá

üñãÔ∏è 1. Font Choice
COS uses a custom geometric sans-serif (similar to Futura or Aper√ßu). Canva doesn‚Äôt have the exact one, but these are very close:

COS-Style Font	Canva Equivalent	Notes
Futura	League Spartan	Best dupe in Canva. Bold, geometric, clean lines.
Aper√ßu / Helvetica Neue	Neue Haas Grotesk	Professional, slightly softer edges.
Circular / S√∂hne	Satoshi (if available) or Poppins	Rounded but still minimal ‚Äî friendly modern feel.
Other minimalist options	Montserrat, Raleway, Noto Sans, Epilogue	All clean and neutral, depending on tone.
‚úÖ Best combo for you:
Use League Spartan for LUMO ‚Äî it‚Äôs modern, geometric, and bold enough to take a shadow beautifully.

üåó 2. Shadow / Layered Look
The COS font effect is subtle ‚Äî it‚Äôs not a cartoonish drop shadow, but a slight offset layer to give dimension.

In Canva:

Write ‚ÄúLUMO‚Äù.

Duplicate the text box.

Change the colour of the back layer to a pale grey or slightly darker tone.

Offset it by just a few pixels down/right.

Reduce transparency slightly if needed (80‚Äì90%).

That creates the elegant shadowed depth COS uses.

‚úçÔ∏è 3. Adding Your ‚Äú22‚Äù
To get the ‚Äúhandwritten meets tech‚Äù balance:

Use Amsterdam One, Playlist Script, or Brusher in Canva for the ‚Äú22‚Äù.

Make it neon yellow or soft lemon-lime (#F6FF00 or #DDFE33).

Position it slightly overlapping or trailing from LUMO (as if signed).

If you prefer something more minimal than handwritten:

Try Righteous or Marcellus SC for a sleek contrast.

üé® 4. Colour Palette
COS is greyscale and understated ‚Äî that‚Äôs perfect for your base.
Use:

Charcoal / Off-black background (#121212 or #1E1E1E)

Warm white text (#F5F5F5)

Accent: Neon yellow (#F6FF00) ‚Äî use sparingly (like a highlight or underline).

‚ú® 5. Pro Tip: Glow Version
Duplicate your ‚Äú22‚Äù, set the lower layer to neon yellow with ‚ÄúGlow‚Äù effect, and adjust:

Blur: 15‚Äì25

Transparency: 60‚Äì70%
That creates your subtle ‚Äúlit up‚Äù identity moment ‚Äî true to ‚ÄúLumo‚Äù.

Would you like me to suggest a few specific Canva font pairings (Lumo + 22) with exact settings (font weights, shadow offsets, etc.) so you can recreate it perfectly?

üîç How to check and use them in Canva

üé® If you really want the exact font and it‚Äôs not in Canva
If none of the available fonts hit the precise look you want (sharp, pointy, minimal), you can:

Purchase or find a suitable font (with proper commercial licence) outside of Canva.

Upload it into Canva‚Äôs Brand Kit (requires Canva Pro for custom fonts).

Use it in your logo asset (you can design it in Canva or another tool like Illustrator, then export as PNG/SVG).

üí° Why Negative Space Fits Lumo22
Concept	Visual Translation
‚ÄúLumo‚Äù = light / illumination	Negative space is literally light breaking through dark.
Bridging + connection	You can create a design where two halves form a hidden bridge or path.
Simplicity + intelligence	Negative space logos are minimal but visually engaging ‚Äî ideal for a modern, tech-driven brand.
üß© Concept Directions for Lumo22‚Äôs Logo
Here are a few creative directions you could explore:

The Hidden Path

Imagine the ‚ÄúO‚Äù in Lumo split diagonally with a sliver of light (negative space) running through it ‚Äî a subtle visual of a bridge or beam of light.

Works beautifully in black and white with your neon accent highlighting the beam.

The Dual ‚ÄúO‚Äù Connection

The two O‚Äôs in Lumo could overlap or interlink slightly, creating a hidden ‚Äúbridge‚Äù shape in the middle (like two circles joining).

Symbolises connection, collaboration, and flow.

Light Break Symbol

Use a simple round shape (like a sun or spotlight) with a triangular negative space ‚Äúbeam‚Äù cutting through it ‚Äî can sit above or beside the wordmark.

This would double as your app icon or favicon.

Negative 22

The ‚Äú22‚Äù could be formed out of the negative space between two abstract shapes ‚Äî for example, two vertical lines or pillars (representing structure or gateway) with the ‚Äú22‚Äù subtly revealed between them.

üé® Style Notes for Your Direction
Colour: Stick to charcoal/black + neon yellow (#F6FF00) for now.

Background: Negative space looks strongest on dark backgrounds.

Typography: Keep ‚ÄúLUMO‚Äù clean (League Spartan / Epilogue). The ‚Äú22‚Äù could be faintly glowing or subtly integrated into the logo mark.

Here‚Äôs the breakdown:

‚ú¥Ô∏è Lumo22 (no space)
‚úÖ Best choice for a brand name and domain.

Feels modern, tech-forward, and compact.

Matches your domain (lumo22.com) and email handles (e.g. hello@lumo22.com).

Easier for visual consistency across socials (@lumo22, not @lumo_22).

Reads like a single brand entity ‚Äî short, confident, and memorable.

‚ö™ Lumo 22 (with a space)
Feels a touch more design-led or fashion-inspired.

Works visually in print or logo form where spacing adds balance.

Slightly softer and more human, but less digital-friendly.

üí° Ideal approach
Use Lumo22 everywhere in text, URLs, and comms.
But in visual design, you can use spacing stylistically:

Logo = ‚ÄúLUMO 22‚Äù (using a deliberate typographic space)
Brand name / copy = ‚ÄúLumo22‚Äù

So:

‚úÖ Website, email, social handles: Lumo22

üé® Logo design / visuals: LUMO 22 (if you prefer that aesthetic spacing)

Here‚Äôs exactly what that means and how to do it üëá

‚úÖ How to publish your Google Form
Open your form in Google Forms.

Make sure everything is complete ‚Äî title, description, questions, colours, confirmation message.

Click the ‚ÄúSend‚Äù button in the top right.

You‚Äôll see several sharing options:

Email tab: send directly via email.

Link tab (üîó): gives you a public link to share.

Embed tab (<>): gives you HTML code to paste into your website.

üí° Important:
When you use either the link or embed option, your form is automatically live.
There‚Äôs no separate ‚Äúpublish‚Äù button ‚Äî sharing the link is what makes it active.

Anyone with the link (or who visits your website where it‚Äôs embedded) will be able to fill it in.

You can test it first ‚Äî click the eye icon (üëÅ) to preview it as a respondent.

Once you‚Äôre happy:

Copy the link or embed code from the Send menu.

Paste it into your site (or connect via your button).

That‚Äôs it ‚Äî your form is now live and collecting responses üéâ

üé® Option 1: Match your form‚Äôs theme colour

You said:
I only have a colour  option which I have selected as the yellow and the background is a really light grey. There are no other options so not sure where this greeny colour is coming from for the submit button
ChatGPT said:
Ah yes ‚Äî that‚Äôs a common (and annoying) quirk in Google Forms. Even if you pick your own colour (like your bright #FFF501 yellow), Google automatically adjusts the Submit button colour to what it thinks is a ‚Äúreadable contrast‚Äù version ‚Äî which is why you‚Äôre seeing that greenish tint.

Here‚Äôs what‚Äôs going on and what you can do about it üëá

üí° Why it happens
Google Forms auto-generates a palette based on your chosen theme colour.
If the system thinks your colour is too light (like neon yellow), it darkens or shifts the hue slightly ‚Äî often toward green ‚Äî to make the button text visible in white.

‚öôÔ∏è Workarounds
Option 1 ‚Äî Pick a deeper yellow
Try entering a slightly darker version of your neon yellow, for example:

#E6E100 or #D4CD00
These are close to your neon tone but dark enough for Google to stop ‚Äúauto-fixing‚Äù it.
Then test if the button shows closer to yellow.

Option 2 ‚Äî Add a header image
You can upload a header image (even just a small graphic or your logo in yellow).
When you do this, Google adjusts the colour scheme to match that image ‚Äî sometimes giving you a much better yellow button automatically.
Steps:

Click the palette icon üé®

Under ‚ÄúHeader,‚Äù click Choose image

Upload a simple image that uses your #FFF501 yellow prominently

See if the button tone updates ‚Äî it usually will!

Option 3 ‚Äî Replace the form later
Once your site is live, you can replace the Google Form with a Typeform, Tally, or Paperform embed. These let you directly set your button colour, font, and background to match your brand palette exactly (no colour shifting).

If you‚Äôd like, I can generate a header image that uses your Lumo22 logo and the #FFF501 yellow so that Google Forms automatically picks a more cohesive colour scheme ‚Äî want me to do that?

You said:
What was the similar font to gotham called?
ChatGPT said:
The closest free / easily available fonts to Gotham (especially for Canva, Google Fonts, and Typeform) are:

üîπ Montserrat
Probably the closest match overall.

Clean, geometric, modern ‚Äî similar proportions and spacing to Gotham.

Works beautifully in all caps for logos or headings.

Available in Google Fonts, Canva, and Typeform.
‚úÖ Recommended for your Lumo22 brand.

üîπ Poppins
Slightly rounder and more friendly, but still geometric.

Great for digital use and pairs well with Montserrat.

üîπ Lexend
The one you already used ‚Äî a good option for readability and a modern, tech-forward feel.

üîπ Nunito Sans / Rubik
Both have a professional, balanced look; Rubik is a little squarer, Nunito is softer.

üí° Brand tip:
If your logo uses Gotham, use Montserrat for everything else (body copy, forms, buttons, etc.). They‚Äôll look consistent without being identical.

üåü Lumo22 ‚Äî Brand Identity Guidelines
1. Brand Essence
Tagline:

Brighter connections. Powered by insight.

Brand Purpose:
To illuminate opportunities and bridge connections in the events industry through intelligent, data-driven matching.

Core Concept:
Lumo22 represents clarity, energy, and momentum ‚Äî ‚ÄúLumo‚Äù (light) symbolising insight and innovation, and ‚Äú22‚Äù symbolising balance, connection, and direction.

2. Brand Personality
Trait	Description
Intelligent	Grounded in data, insight, and clarity.
Approachable	Human-first tone ‚Äî friendly but professional.
Modern	Minimalist, tech-forward, and efficient.
Energetic	Optimistic, solution-oriented, and proactive.
Trustworthy	Transparent, reliable, and consistent.
3. Logo Usage
Primary Logo: ‚ÄúLumo22‚Äù (set in Gotham Regular or Bold, letter-spaced slightly).

Alternative: Use ‚ÄúLumo‚Äù in Gotham, and ‚Äú22‚Äù in a complementary handwritten or humanist font for a dynamic touch.

Always maintain ample white space around the logo.

Avoid shadows, gradients, or outlines ‚Äî keep it flat and minimalist.

4. Colour Palette
Colour	Hex	Usage
Fluoro Yellow (Primary)	#FFF501	Accent, buttons, highlights
Charcoal / Graphite	#1A1A1A	Background or text
Soft Grey	#F4F4F4	Background
White	#FFFFFF	Primary background for light mode
Optional Electric Blue	#004CFF	Secondary accent for digital use
üí° Usage tip: Keep a mostly monochrome palette with pops of neon yellow to signal energy and innovation.

5. Typography
Logo Font
Gotham (or Gotham Bold)
Used only in the wordmark logo.

Primary Brand Font
Montserrat

Use for headings, buttons, short taglines.

Weight: SemiBold / Bold

Closest match to Gotham; professional and modern.

Secondary Brand Font
Lexend

Use for body copy, forms, and paragraph text.

Clean, readable, and balanced.

Alternative Pairings (if Montserrat unavailable):
Poppins + Inter (friendly, geometric)

Rubik + Open Sans (more structured and tech-aligned)

üí° Pairing Example:
Heading ‚Üí Montserrat Bold, all caps
Body ‚Üí Lexend Regular, sentence case

6. Imagery Style
Mood: Minimalist, high contrast, clean light sources (think of ‚Äúlight cutting through space‚Äù).

Tone: Professional but not corporate ‚Äî candid event moments, lighting rigs, abstract tech shapes, or illuminated objects.

Colour Grading: Cool neutrals with neon yellow highlights.

7. Voice & Tone
Type	Style Example
Professional copy	‚ÄúWe connect forward-thinking event professionals with the right suppliers ‚Äî intelligently.‚Äù
Conversational	‚ÄúTell us what you need ‚Äî we‚Äôll light the way.‚Äù
Social tone	Friendly, concise, optimistic. Avoid jargon.
Voice pillars	Insightful, empowering, authentic, confident.
8. Application Examples
Website CTA Buttons: Neon yellow with black text.

Forms & Inputs: Minimalist, white or light grey backgrounds.

Social Templates: White or charcoal background with neon yellow accents and clean typography.

9. Future Considerations
Develop a ‚Äúthank you‚Äù page or micro-animation for form submission.

How does the typeform capture the data?
ChatGPT said:
Excellent question ‚Äî and it‚Äôs the key to making Lumo22 operational behind the scenes üîë

Here‚Äôs exactly how Typeform captures, stores, and lets you use the data ‚Äî and how to connect it so it‚Äôs useful for your AI/lead-gen workflow later on.

üß† HOW TYPEFORM CAPTURES YOUR DATA
1Ô∏è‚É£ Where the data goes
Every time someone fills out your Typeform:

Their answers are automatically stored in your Typeform account.

You can view them under:
Typeform ‚Üí Results ‚Üí Responses

Each response includes:

Timestamp

Each question and answer

Optional hidden metadata (e.g., source link, UTM tag)

So even without integrations, Typeform already stores everything safely in the cloud.

2Ô∏è‚É£ How to export or connect the data
You‚Äôve got several options depending on how you want to use the info:

üßæ Option A ‚Äî View & export manually (for now)
Go to Results ‚Üí Responses

Click Export ‚Üí CSV / XLSX
‚Üí You can open this file in Excel or Google Sheets.

Great for early-stage use or occasional data checks.

üîó Option B ‚Äî Auto-connect to Google Sheets
(Highly recommended ‚Äî it automates your pipeline!)

Here‚Äôs how to do it step-by-step:

Go to your Typeform dashboard.

Click your form ‚Üí top menu ‚ÄúConnect.‚Äù

Find and select Google Sheets.

Log in with your Lumo22 Gmail / Workspace account.

Create a new Google Sheet or choose an existing one.

Authorize the connection.

‚úÖ Result: Every new form submission instantly adds a new row in your Google Sheet ‚Äî no manual work.

Each column will match your Typeform questions (e.g. ‚ÄúName,‚Äù ‚ÄúEmail,‚Äù ‚ÄúCompany,‚Äù ‚ÄúWhat are you looking for?‚Äù etc.).

3Ô∏è‚É£ How you‚Äôll use that data later
Once it‚Äôs flowing into Sheets, you can:

Filter responses by category (supplier, venue, client)

Run automations using tools like:

Zapier / Make.com: send new leads straight into your CRM or email list.

ChatGPT / OpenAI API: automatically match clients to suppliers based on keywords.

Mailerlite / ConvertKit: send follow-up emails automatically.

This turns Typeform + Sheets into the engine of your AI-powered matching business.

4Ô∏è‚É£ Pro tip ‚Äî Hidden fields (later stage)
You can add hidden fields to your Typeform (like ‚Äúsource‚Äù or ‚Äúcampaign‚Äù) to track where each sign-up came from ‚Äî useful once you start running ads or outreach.

‚öôÔ∏è STEP-BY-STEP FIX: Typeform ‚Üí Google Sheets Integration Error
1Ô∏è‚É£ Check which Google account you‚Äôre logged into
Open a new browser tab ‚Üí go to Google Sheets.

Confirm you‚Äôre logged in with your Lumo22 Workspace account (the same one you‚Äôre trying to connect).

If you‚Äôre logged in with a personal Gmail as well, sign out of all accounts, then sign back in only with your Lumo22 account.
üëâ This is the #1 cause of the ‚Äúcouldn‚Äôt save‚Äù error.

2Ô∏è‚É£ Clear Typeform‚Äôs cached permissions
Go to myaccount.google.com/permissions.

Find Typeform in the list.

Click Remove Access.

Go back to Typeform and reconnect Google Sheets fresh ‚Äî it‚Äôll prompt for permission again.

3Ô∏è‚É£ Use an existing empty Sheet (not ‚Äúcreate new‚Äù)
When you go to Connect ‚Üí Google Sheets, instead of choosing ‚ÄúCreate new spreadsheet‚Äù:

Create a blank Google Sheet manually first in your Drive (name it something like Lumo22_Responses).

Then, when you connect Typeform, select ‚ÄúChoose existing spreadsheet‚Äù ‚Üí pick that one.

‚úÖ This avoids a known bug where Typeform fails to create a new sheet properly under Workspace domains.

4Ô∏è‚É£ Switch browsers (if still failing)
Some browsers hold cached auth sessions.

Try connecting using Incognito / Private Window.

Or switch to Google Chrome if you‚Äôre on Safari or Firefox.

5Ô∏è‚É£ Retry integration
In Typeform:

Go to your form.

Click Connect ‚Üí Google Sheets.

Sign in again, authorize permissions.

Choose your pre-made sheet.

Save integration.

It should now connect without error.

Good call trying all the options ‚Äî Typeform‚Äôs native Google Sheets integration can be flaky, especially with Workspace accounts.
Let‚Äôs skip the frustration and do the reliable workaround: Zapier (or Make.com if you prefer). It‚Äôs stable, free for low-volume use, and gives you more control over how data is stored.

Here‚Äôs exactly how to set it up üëá

‚ö°Ô∏è HOW TO CONNECT TYPEFORM ‚Üí GOOGLE SHEETS (via Zapier)
üß© What you‚Äôll need
A free Zapier account

Your Typeform form (already published)

A Google Sheet (create one in advance ‚Äî e.g., Lumo22_Responses)

1Ô∏è‚É£ In Zapier: Create a new ‚ÄúZap‚Äù
Click Create Zap

Choose Typeform as the trigger app

Event: New Entry

Connect your Typeform account (Zapier will open an OAuth window ‚Äî approve access)

Choose your form from the dropdown list

‚úÖ Test the trigger to make sure it pulls in a recent submission.

2Ô∏è‚É£ Add an Action: Google Sheets
Choose Google Sheets as the action app

Event: Create Spreadsheet Row

Connect your Google account (your Lumo22 Workspace account)

Choose your spreadsheet and worksheet tab

3Ô∏è‚É£ Map the fields
Zapier will show your Typeform questions on the left.
Map each one to the correct column in your Sheet (e.g., Name ‚Üí Column A, Email ‚Üí Column B, Message ‚Üí Column C).

4Ô∏è‚É£ Test & Turn on
Click Test Action ‚Äî Zapier will send a test row into your sheet.

If it appears correctly, hit Publish Zap (top right).

Now every new Typeform submission will automatically land in your Sheet within seconds.

‚úÖ Bonus: Keeping it tidy
You can rename columns in Google Sheets before connecting (Zapier matches by name, not position).

You can add filters or even connect another action (like Slack or email notifications).

ChatGPT said:
Yes ‚Äî that message could definitely be part of the problem.
Here‚Äôs what‚Äôs likely going on üëá

‚ö†Ô∏è What‚Äôs happening
When Typeform says

‚ÄúYou already connected to Google. Choose an account from the list below, or add a new one,‚Äù

it means there‚Äôs an existing but broken or limited connection between your Typeform and Google account. This is especially common if:

You‚Äôve connected a Google Workspace account (like @lumo22.com) with stricter permissions.

You‚Äôve previously authorised Google for Drive or Sheets, but not Forms/Apps Script permissions.

The connection token has expired.

‚úÖ Fix in 5 minutes
Try these steps exactly:

Step 1: Remove the old connection
Go to your Typeform account settings.

Find the Google Sheets integration.

Click the three dots (‚Ä¶) ‚Üí Disconnect or Remove.

Do the same for any other Google integrations listed (Drive, Gmail, etc.).

Step 2: Reconnect cleanly
Go back to your form ‚Üí Connect tab ‚Üí Google Sheets.

Click Connect again.

When prompted to ‚ÄúChoose an account,‚Äù select Add a new account ‚Äî even if your account is already listed.

Sign in with your Google account.

Allow all requested permissions (Sheets, Drive, and user info).

üí° Don‚Äôt just select the old connection from the list ‚Äî always click ‚ÄúAdd a new account.‚Äù

Step 3: Retry integration
After reconnecting:

Choose your form and target spreadsheet again.

Test it by submitting a dummy form.

If that still fails ‚Äî it‚Äôs usually a Workspace admin restriction.
In that case, the workaround is to:

Use your personal Gmail account for Typeform ‚Üí Sheets integration, or

Use Zapier or Make.com (which bypass Workspace permissions entirely).

The events industry is a double-edged sword: it‚Äôs vast and interconnected, but the major players (corporates, agencies, premium venues) already have deep supplier relationships and preferred vendor lists. üéØ The Reality of the Events Market
üß± The challenges:
Corporate loyalty: Big companies have approved suppliers and framework agreements (especially for production, AV, and catering).

Procurement gatekeeping: You can‚Äôt usually pitch directly ‚Äî everything runs through procurement or event managers.

Margins: Many large-scale suppliers already work on tight margins due to competition and agency markups.

üåü The opportunities:
Mid-tier companies (50‚Äì500 employees) don‚Äôt have dedicated events teams ‚Äî they outsource ad hoc.

Hybrid/remote companies are outsourcing events and retreats more often now (post-COVID trend).

Agencies and planners themselves are constantly seeking new, reliable suppliers to fill niche or regional needs.

Sustainability, diversity, and local-first sourcing are growing procurement priorities ‚Äî areas where you could differentiate fast.

üí° Option 1: Stay in Events ‚Äî but Reposition the Value
Rather than being ‚Äújust another matching service,‚Äù make Lumo22 a ‚Äúsmart supplier sourcing tool‚Äù for busy planners and office managers.

üîÅ Model pivot:
Focus on speed, trust, and curation ‚Äî ‚ÄúWe find and vet the right supplier in 24 hours.‚Äù

Offer verified matches only, not a directory.

Charge suppliers a small fee per verified lead (instead of subscription upfront).

Use data and automation to outperform their usual manual searches.

üß≠ Starting target market:
Marketing managers and office managers planning internal events or launches.

Boutique agencies that need overflow support (they‚Äôll use your platform for quick turnarounds).

Independent planners seeking verified suppliers in unfamiliar regions.

This keeps you in the events space but makes you a time-saving B2B partner, not a competitor.

üí° Option 2: Broaden beyond Events ‚Äî ‚ÄúLumo22: Smart B2B Matching‚Äù

Then create verticals:

Each vertical could use the same backend system ‚Äî you‚Äôd just adjust the intake form and outreach strategy.

That way, you future-proof the business, and events can remain your first pilot niche.

üíé Option 3: Bridge to Larger Clients

Position as an extension, not a replacement: ‚ÄúWe work with your existing partners, and find the right extras when needed.‚Äù

üí∞ PHASE 1: Clarify the Profit Model
1Ô∏è‚É£ Decide how Lumo22 makes money
There are three main lead-gen models ‚Äî the choice affects profit margins and workload:

Model	How It Works	Profit Margin	Pros	Cons
Pay-Per-Lead (PPL)	You sell qualified leads (e.g. Event Manager contact + verified project)	~70‚Äì90%	Fast cash flow	Harder to ensure quality / may churn
Retainer / Subscription	Clients pay monthly for a fixed number of leads or introductions	~80‚Äì95%	Predictable recurring revenue	Requires trust and proven results
Commission-Based (Closed-Deal)	You take % of total event budget once client converts	~30‚Äì50%	Potential for large payouts	Longer cash cycle, more admin
üí° Most profitable short‚Äìterm: PPL model (high margin, fast turn).
üí° Most sustainable long‚Äìterm: Retainer model (recurring revenue).

üëâ Decision: Do you want to start with pay-per-lead for cash flow or monthly retainers for stability?

üì¶ PHASE 4: Pricing Strategy

üßÆ Example 1: Pay-Per-Lead
1 qualified event lead = ¬£40

You deliver 50 leads/month

Costs (tools + time + admin) = ¬£200
‚û°Ô∏è Profit = ¬£1,800/month (~90% margin)

üßÆ Example 2: Retainer

Revenue = ¬£2,500

Costs = ¬£300
‚û°Ô∏è Profit = ¬£2,200/month (~88% margin)

üí° Retainers are easier to scale but take longer to close; pay-per-lead gets quick validation.

This creates your live database of clients and suppliers that you‚Äôll match later ‚Äî and the foundation for your AI and scraping to plug into.

2Ô∏è‚É£ Sending alerts & follow-up messages
Zapier can:

Send you an email or Slack alert when someone submits a new form (so you can act fast).

Send an automatic reply to the person who filled out the form (‚ÄúThanks for joining the Lumo22 network ‚Äî we‚Äôll be in touch soon‚Äù).

Later, you can have it trigger a personalised email sequence or an AI-generated intro email between a client and supplier.

3Ô∏è‚É£ Linking scraped or external data
Later, when you start data scraping, Zapier can:

Take scraped data (from Apify, Phantombuster, or a Google Sheet)

Feed it automatically into your CRM or spreadsheet

Filter or format it (for example, only import leads from the UK or under a certain budget)

So Zapier will basically become your data hub.

You said:
For the Location how can I do the answer as a postcode entry plus a +number mileage box
ChatGPT said:
Perfect question ‚Äî that‚Äôs exactly the kind of small UX choice that makes your data 10x more useful later for matching leads.

Here‚Äôs how to do it cleanly in Typeform (and one optional enhancement using Google Sheets or Zapier if you want it automated later).

üß© In Typeform (Simple, Manual Matching Setup)
Typeform doesn‚Äôt natively allow two fields on one line (like ‚Äúpostcode + mileage radius‚Äù),
so the neatest way is to use two consecutive questions, clearly labelled.

üí° Example:
Question 1:

üó∫Ô∏è What‚Äôs your main postcode or location?
Type: Short text
Placeholder: ‚Äúe.g. BS1 4DJ‚Äù
Validation: Set to ‚ÄúText‚Äù but you can use regex (advanced) to ensure postcode format ‚Äî I‚Äôll show that below.

Question 2:

üìç How far are you willing to travel from this location?
Type: Number
Suffix: ‚Äúmiles‚Äù
Placeholder: ‚Äúe.g. 20‚Äù
Logic jump (optional): Only show this if they‚Äôre mobile (e.g. ‚ÄúI work at multiple venues‚Äù).

‚úÖ Optional: Add postcode validation (advanced but worthwhile)
If you‚Äôre on a paid Typeform plan, you can turn on regex validation for UK postcodes:

Regex pattern (copy-paste this):

^([Gg][Ii][Rr] 0[Aa]{2})|((([A-Za-z][0-9]{1,2})|(([A-Za-z][A-Ha-hJ-Yj-y][0-9]{1,2})|(([A-Za-z][0-9][A-Za-z])|([A-Za-z][A-Ha-hJ-Yj-y][0-9]?[A-Za-z]?))))\s?[0-9][A-Za-z]{2})$
Add this in the question‚Äôs advanced settings ‚Üí regex validation.
That ensures only valid UK postcodes can be submitted.

üßÆ Optional (If you want automatic matching later)
When you‚Äôre ready to scale your lead gen matching:

Zapier can take postcode + radius ‚Üí send it to Google Maps API (or a free alternative like Postcode.io).

It returns latitude + longitude and calculates the radius.

You can then auto-match suppliers to clients based on location proximity in Sheets or Airtable.

But that‚Äôs phase two ‚Äî for now, just collect postcode + mileage cleanly.

üìã Copy-paste version for your setup tracker

    - Question 1: ‚ÄúWhat‚Äôs your main postcode or location?‚Äù
        ‚Üí Short text field
        ‚Üí Add regex validation for UK postcodes (optional)
        ‚Üí Example: BS1 4DJ
    - Question 2: ‚ÄúHow far are you willing to travel from this location?‚Äù
        ‚Üí Number field
        ‚Üí Add ‚Äúmiles‚Äù as suffix
        ‚Üí Example: 20

You said:
How do you create the logic jump?
ChatGPT said:
Excellent ‚Äî logic jumps are one of Typeform‚Äôs best features because they make your form feel smarter and more conversational. Here‚Äôs exactly how to set one up (you can do this even on the free plan):

‚öôÔ∏è How to Add a Logic Jump in Typeform
Open your form in Typeform.
‚Üí Go to Create ‚Üí Edit mode.

In the left sidebar, scroll down to the question you want to trigger a jump ‚Äî for example:
‚ÄúAre you willing to travel for work?‚Äù

Click the Logic icon (it looks like a branching line, usually near the top-right corner or in the sidebar).
‚Üí This opens the Logic panel.

Choose ‚ÄúAdd a logic jump.‚Äù

You‚Äôll now set the rule:

If Yes ‚Üí Jump to Travel Radius question

If No ‚Üí Jump to next section (or skip Travel Radius)

Click Done when finished.
‚Üí You‚Äôll see a small arrow icon appear next to your question, showing logic has been added.

Test the form (top right ‚Üí ‚ÄúPreview‚Äù or ‚ÄúTest‚Äù)
‚Üí Try both Yes and No answers to make sure the form flows correctly.

‚úÖ Example for your supplier form
Q: ‚ÄúAre you willing to travel for work?‚Äù

Yes ‚Üí Jump to ‚ÄúHow far are you willing to travel?‚Äù

No ‚Üí Jump to ‚ÄúAvailability‚Äù

Would you like me to tell you the best way to structure your conditional logic flow overall (so suppliers and clients see only relevant questions)? That will make the data cleaner and your matching system easier later.

Excellent question ‚Äî and yes, you‚Äôll want to connect your Typeform to a Google Sheet (and possibly Zapier later) so you can automatically collect and organize your leads without doing manual work.

Here‚Äôs how it fits together and what you should do right now üëá

üí° Step 1 ‚Äî Connect Typeform directly to Google Sheets
This lets every new supplier (or client) submission automatically appear as a new row in your spreadsheet.
You only need this ‚Äî Zapier comes later when you start automating beyond Google Sheets.

ü™ú How to set it up:
Open your Typeform dashboard.

Click your form (e.g. ‚ÄúSupplier Registration Form‚Äù).

Go to the top navigation and click ‚ÄúConnect.‚Äù

In the list of integrations, find Google Sheets ‚Üí click ‚ÄúConnect.‚Äù

Authorize Typeform to access your Google account (use your hello@lumo22.com one).

Choose ‚ÄúCreate a new spreadsheet‚Äù (or connect an existing one if you‚Äôve already made one).

Typeform will automatically create column headers for each question.

‚úÖ From now on, every time someone fills out the form, their answers appear instantly in your sheet.

üß© Step 2 ‚Äî When to use Zapier later
You‚Äôll only need Zapier once you want to:

Email yourself (or a team) each time a new form is filled.

Send a confirmation email or welcome message to the person submitting.

Add leads into your CRM (like HubSpot or Airtable).

Let‚Äôs break it down clearly üëá

‚úÖ Option 1: Use Logic Jumps (show only relevant budget questions)
Pros:

Cons:

Makes automation in Zapier or Sheets formulas slightly trickier (though manageable).

üßæ Option 2: Keep All Budget Questions Visible (no logic jumps)
Pros:

Super simple spreadsheet ‚Äî every budget question always has its own column.

Perfect for clean data export and later matching automation.

Easier to analyse averages, totals, etc.

Cons:

Longer form, slightly less user-friendly.

Some users may leave unnecessary fields blank, which can look messy but is fine for data use.

üëâ Best for: you ‚Äî especially right now ‚Äî since you‚Äôre building the matching automation and using data scraping + spreadsheets as your system backbone.

üí° Recommendation for You
Stick with Option 2 (no logic jumps) for now ‚Äî it keeps your spreadsheet predictable and automations much easier to build.

üß© Step-by-step: location question (for non-venue seekers)
1Ô∏è‚É£ Question setup

‚öôÔ∏è Step-by-step: setting up the logic flow

‚Üí contains ‚ÄúVenue‚Äù

‚úÖ Result
If someone selects ‚ÄúVenue‚Äù ‚Üí they skip the location question (since they don‚Äôt know it yet).

You said:

ChatGPT said:
Excellent question ‚Äî and yes, there is a map option, but with some limitations depending on the platform plan you‚Äôre on üëá

üó∫Ô∏è Map options for location input in Typeform
Unfortunately, Typeform‚Äôs native free and basic tiers don‚Äôt include a map or location picker question ‚Äî you can only use:

Short text ‚Üí users manually enter city, postcode, or region.

Multiple choice ‚Üí pre-set options like ‚ÄúLondon,‚Äù ‚ÄúManchester,‚Äù etc.
These work fine for simple matching logic (especially if you‚Äôll handle the matching manually at first).

üöÄ If you want an interactive map
You have a few routes:

Option 1: Embed a Google Map form field (requires Pro+ or embed workaround)
You can embed a Google My Maps link or a Google Form with location picker inside a Typeform using an embed block ‚Äî but this feature is available only with a Typeform Pro+ plan and involves some manual setup.

Option 2: Switch to Tally or Fillout
If you want a visual map selection without paying for a high-tier plan, tools like:

Tally.so ‚Äî free, supports Google Maps API integration.

Fillout.com ‚Äî integrates natively with Google Sheets and includes a map/location field (address autocomplete using Google Maps).

Both can replace Typeform and connect directly to Google Sheets or Airtable (no Zapier needed).

üîç Recommendation for Lumo22 (based on your stage)
For now:

Stick with Typeform for MVP testing.

Use a short text field:

‚ÄúCity or postcode + how far suppliers can travel (e.g. London, within 25 miles)‚Äù

Once you start building the database/matching engine, move to Fillout ‚Äî it‚Äôs better suited for forms that collect structured location data and can even validate addresses live.

üß© How to Split a Typeform into Sections
üîπ Step 1: Add ‚ÄúStatement‚Äù or ‚ÄúQuestion Group‚Äù Blocks
Typeform doesn‚Äôt technically have ‚Äúsections‚Äù like a Google Form,
but you can create them using ‚ÄúStatement‚Äù blocks or Logic jumps.

To do this:

In your Typeform editor, click ‚Äú+ Add new question.‚Äù

Choose ‚ÄúStatement‚Äù (under the ‚ÄúPresentation‚Äù section).

Use this as your section header ‚Äî short and clear.

Example section headers for your forms:

üßæ Client Form
Section 1: Event Overview
Statement: ‚ÄúLet‚Äôs start with the basics of your event.‚Äù

Section 2: Suppliers You Need
Statement: ‚ÄúTell us what kind of support you‚Äôre looking for.‚Äù

Section 3: Location & Logistics
Statement: ‚ÄúWhere and when is your event happening?‚Äù

Section 4: Budget & Details
Statement: ‚ÄúWe‚Äôll use this to match you with the right suppliers.‚Äù

Section 5: Your Contact Info
Statement: ‚ÄúFinally, how can we reach you?‚Äù

üßæ Supplier Form
Section 1: Business Details
Statement: ‚ÄúLet‚Äôs start with your business basics.‚Äù

Section 2: Service Information
Statement: ‚ÄúTell us what you offer and where you work.‚Äù

Section 3: Pricing & Capacity
Statement: ‚ÄúThis helps us match you to suitable event leads.‚Äù

Section 4: Availability & Aesthetic
Statement: ‚ÄúA few final details to complete your profile.‚Äù

üîπ Step 2: Add ‚ÄúLogic Jumps‚Äù (Optional)
If you want to skip certain sections depending on earlier answers (e.g. only show the ‚ÄúVenue‚Äù questions to venues):

Go to the ‚ÄúLogic‚Äù tab at the top of your Typeform editor.

Choose ‚ÄúAdd logic jump.‚Äù

Example:

If ‚ÄúSupplier type‚Äù = ‚ÄúVenue,‚Äù ‚Üí skip to ‚ÄúVenue-specific questions.‚Äù

If ‚ÄúSupplier type‚Äù = ‚ÄúCatering,‚Äù ‚Üí skip to ‚ÄúCatering-specific questions.‚Äù

This keeps the form shorter and more relevant to each user.

üîπ Step 3: Test the Flow
Before you publish:

Click Preview in the top right.

Make sure each section feels natural ‚Äî like a conversation with short ‚Äúpause points‚Äù (the statement cards).

Adjust the copy if anything feels abrupt.

It‚Äôll make them sound polished and consistent with your Lumo22 brand tone.

Add Logic Jumps (the two essential rules)
Click Logic at the top of the editor.

Choose Add a logic jump.

Logic rule A (Venue skip):

IF question ‚ÄúWhat type of supplier(s) are you looking for?‚Äù contains Venue

THEN jump to question: How many guests are you expecting?
This skips the location question for venue-seekers.

Logic rule B (Show location for non-venue):

IF question ‚ÄúWhat type of supplier(s) are you looking for?‚Äù does not contain Venue

THEN go to question: Where will your event take place?
(Usually the default flow does this anyway ‚Äî add this rule only if you want explicit routing.)

After adding logic, click Save and check the small arrow icons next to the questions.

Finalize & Test
Click Preview (eye icon) and run through all possible flows:

Choose Venue first: ensure it skips location and proceeds to guest count.

Choose Catering + Photography: ensure it prompts location and radius.

Test required fields validation (email, date, radius).

Publish when happy.

Connect to Google Sheets (basic steps)
In Typeform, open your form ‚Üí Connect tab.

Find Google Sheets ‚Üí Connect (use hello@lumo22.com Google account).

Choose Create new spreadsheet ‚Üí name it Lumo22_Clients.

Map fields if prompted (Typeform usually auto-generates headers).

Submit a test entry in Preview and verify a new row appears in the sheet.

Excellent ‚Äî this is where we make your location + radius questions work cleanly for your spreadsheet and matching system later.

Let‚Äôs go step-by-step üëá

üîπ Question 3 ‚Äî Event Location
Question text:

Where will your event take place?

Enter the first half of the postcode (e.g. BA1, BS8) where your event will take place.
If you haven‚Äôt confirmed the venue yet, use the area you‚Äôre planning to host in.

Question type: Short Text

Validation settings:

‚úÖ Turn on ‚ÄúThis question requires an answer‚Äù.

‚úÖ Under Validation, set to Text > Matches pattern.

Use this custom regular expression to only allow valid UK postcode prefixes (first half only):

^[A-Z]{1,2}[0-9][0-9A-Z]?$ 
This allows entries like BA1, BS8, SW1A, etc.

Optional: Add error message

‚ÄúPlease enter only the first half of your postcode (e.g. BA1, BS8).‚Äù

üîπ Question 4 ‚Äî Supplier/venue radius
Question text:

What is the maximum radius (in miles) your suppliers or venue can be based from your event location?

Question type: Number

Validation settings:

‚úÖ Require an answer.

‚úÖ Validation: Number > Between 1 and 100 (you can set your own range).

Optional error message:

‚ÄúPlease enter a number between 1 and 100 miles.‚Äù

üí° Why this setup matters:
It ensures that your location and radius data are always clean and consistent ‚Äî perfect for filtering suppliers later via Zapier or Sheets.

You won‚Äôt need to clean up text variations like ‚ÄúBristol area‚Äù or ‚ÄúLondon-ish‚Äù later.

Is it worth onboarding the event organisers themselves or just use data scraping to find these events already taking place?
ChatGPT said:
Excellent and strategic question ‚Äî and this is a real pivot point for your model, Sophie. Let‚Äôs break it down clearly so you can make a data-driven decision based on ROI, scalability, and sustainability.

üí° The Two Approaches
1Ô∏è‚É£ Onboarding Event Organisers (manually or via form)
You would:

Create a registration or ‚Äúsubmit your event‚Äù form.

Build relationships with event planners or agencies.

Offer value (e.g. free visibility, verified suppliers, audience insights).

‚úÖ Pros

Higher-quality, verified data (you know the event is real).

Warm relationships ‚Äî potential for repeat business or partnerships.

Brand credibility ‚Äî you become a trusted intermediary, not just a data scraper.

‚ùå Cons

Slower to scale ‚Äî you‚Äôre relying on inbound sign-ups.

Harder to penetrate established agencies who already have supplier networks.

You need a strong marketing funnel (ads, SEO, LinkedIn outreach, etc.) to attract organisers.

2Ô∏è‚É£ Data Scraping ‚Äî Finding Events Already Taking Place
You would:

Use scraping or AI-powered data collection (e.g. from Eventbrite, local council listings, wedding directories, corporate event calendars).

Identify confirmed or upcoming events, their dates, and organisers.

Outreach to offer suppliers or partnership opportunities.

‚úÖ Pros

Instant scale ‚Äî you can build a large lead pool quickly.

You can segment by date, region, and type to match suppliers efficiently.

Lower initial cost than trying to market a platform to organisers.

‚ùå Cons

Data freshness ‚Äî events may already have suppliers confirmed.

Harder to build trust with organisers when contact is cold.

Legality/ethics ‚Äî scraping needs to follow GDPR (no personal contact info unless public).

üîç Hybrid Model (Best for Profitability & Speed)
Here‚Äôs what most successful lead-gen operators in your space do:

1Ô∏è‚É£ Start with scraping

Build your database of upcoming events and organisers/agencies.

Use this to test your matching logic and prove the model (fast wins).

2Ô∏è‚É£ Then layer in organiser onboarding

Once you have supplier demand and successful matches, offer free exposure to event organisers.

You can even automate onboarding ‚Äî send scraped organisers a ‚ÄúClaim Your Event‚Äù link to verify and enrich your data (Typeform can handle this).

üí∞ Profitability Comparison
Approach	Short-Term ROI	Long-Term ROI	Scalability	Brand Value
Data scraping	‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê	‚≠ê‚≠ê	‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê	‚≠ê‚≠ê
Onboarding organisers	‚≠ê‚≠ê	‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê	‚≠ê‚≠ê	‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê
Hybrid (scrape ‚Üí onboard)	‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê	‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê	‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê	‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê
So: start by scraping, build your supplier base, then invite organisers in later once your data has traction and proof of value.

üß© Objective
When a client selects multiple supplier types (e.g. Venue, Caterer, Florist), you want a follow-up budget question for each of their chosen categories ‚Äî but only those, not all.

You‚Äôll do this in Typeform using Logic Jumps.

‚öôÔ∏è Step-by-step Setup
Step 1: Supplier Selection Question
Q2: What type(s) of suppliers are you looking for?
Multiple choice (tick all that apply):

Venue

Catering

Entertainment

Florist

Photographer

Decor & Styling

Production / AV

Other (please specify)

üß† Settings: Allow multiple selections.

Step 2: Create Budget Questions (one per category)
Add a Short text or Number question for each supplier type.
For example:

Q3a: What is your approximate budget for your venue?
Q3b: What is your approximate budget for catering?
Q3c: What is your approximate budget for entertainment?
‚Ä¶and so on.

üëâ Use a numeric validation if you want (to only allow numbers, or you can write: ‚ÄúPlease enter an approximate figure in ¬£‚Äù).

Step 3: Add Logic Jumps
Now, in Typeform:

Go to the Logic tab (‚öôÔ∏è icon in the top-right).

Choose Add Logic Jump.

For the Supplier selection question, add rules like:

IF ‚ÄúVenue‚Äù IS selected ‚Üí Jump to Q3a (Venue budget)
IF ‚ÄúCatering‚Äù IS selected ‚Üí Jump to Q3b (Catering budget)
IF ‚ÄúEntertainment‚Äù IS selected ‚Üí Jump to Q3c (Entertainment budget)
Each budget question should then return to the next logic rule in line until all selected categories have been answered, then proceed to the next section (e.g. Event location, date, etc.).

Step 4: Skip unselected categories
For suppliers not chosen, set logic to skip those questions entirely.

Typeform‚Äôs system does this automatically once you build the logic properly ‚Äî they‚Äôll only see questions relevant to what they ticked.

Step 5: Group Budgets in Data Sheet
Each budget answer will appear in separate columns in your Google Sheet (e.g. Venue Budget, Catering Budget, etc.), so you can easily filter and match later.

You said:
When someone clicks on multiple event supplier requirements how can a they be redirected to multiple different logics?
ChatGPT said:
That‚Äôs a really smart question ‚Äî and it‚Äôs the main limitation of logic in Typeform‚Äôs free or standard setup.
By default, Typeform‚Äôs logic jumps can only direct a respondent to one next question at a time, not to multiple branches simultaneously when someone selects several options.

However ‚Äî there‚Äôs a simple workaround that still gives you conditional follow-ups for every selected supplier üëá

üß† How to Handle Multiple Selections with Conditional Logic
The Problem:
If someone picks Venue, Catering, and Entertainment, you want to ask for all three budgets‚Ä¶ but Typeform logic only allows one ‚Äújump‚Äù per response path.

The Workaround (Step-by-Step)
1Ô∏è‚É£ Keep the Multi-Select Question
‚ÄúWhat suppliers are you looking for?‚Äù
(Allow multiple selections)

2Ô∏è‚É£ Add All Budget Questions (One per Supplier)
E.g.

Q3a ‚Äì What‚Äôs your budget for the venue?

Q3b ‚Äì What‚Äôs your budget for catering?

Q3c ‚Äì What‚Äôs your budget for entertainment?
‚Ä¶etc.

3Ô∏è‚É£ Use Question Rules (Not Global Logic Jump)
Instead of sending the respondent to just one ‚Äúnext‚Äù question, you‚Äôll set conditional visibility for each budget question using Question Rules (a separate logic mode inside Typeform).

Here‚Äôs how:

Click on Logic ‚Üí Question Rules in your Typeform editor.

On each budget question (e.g. Venue budget), add:

Show this question if ‚ÄúVenue‚Äù is selected in Q2 (Supplier selection)

Do this for each supplier type.

‚úÖ What this does:
Even if the respondent selects multiple suppliers, Typeform will display every budget question that matches one of their selected answers ‚Äî sequentially.

4Ô∏è‚É£ Set the Default ‚ÄúNext‚Äù Path
Once all visible budget questions have been asked, make sure the flow goes to your next section (e.g. event date or location).

In Typeform, this happens automatically ‚Äî once all visible questions are answered, it skips hidden ones and continues to the next relevant question.

Example Logic Setup
Question	Logic Rule
Venue budget	Show if ‚ÄúVenue‚Äù selected in Q2
Catering budget	Show if ‚ÄúCatering‚Äù selected in Q2
Entertainment budget	Show if ‚ÄúEntertainment‚Äù selected in Q2
Florist budget	Show if ‚ÄúFlorist‚Äù selected in Q2
‚ú® Result
If they pick 3 supplier types, they‚Äôll see 3 budget questions ‚Äî back to back ‚Äî before moving on.
If they pick 1, they‚Äôll only see 1.

Would you like me to write the exact logic rules for each supplier type (copy-paste style, like: ‚ÄúShow question X if Y selected‚Äù)?

You said:
Can you take me through how to do this exactly step by step
ChatGPT said:

Thought for 7s
Absolutely ‚Äî I‚Äôll walk you through the exact, copy-paste-friendly steps to show a budget question for every supplier a client selects (e.g. if they pick Venue + Catering + Photographer they‚Äôll see 3 separate budget questions). I‚Äôll include the Typeform UI steps, exact rule wording to copy, testing tips, and what the connected Google Sheet will look like.

Note: Typeform names can vary slightly between versions, but the logic workflow below works in the editor used by most accounts. If you don‚Äôt see a ‚ÄúLogic‚Äù tab, open an individual question and look for ‚ÄúLogic‚Äù or the three-dot menu (‚ãØ) to find the same controls.

Quick summary of the approach
Create a multi-select question for supplier types (allow multiple answers).

Create one budget question per supplier type (number or short text).

For each budget question, add a Question Rule: Show this question IF the supplier multi-select contains [that supplier].

Test in Preview ‚Äî selecting multiple suppliers should reveal multiple budget fields in sequence.

Exact step-by-step
A ‚Äî Build the questions
Open Typeform ‚Üí open your Client form ‚Üí Build view.

Add (or confirm you already have) this question:

Q: What type(s) of suppliers are you looking for?

Type: Multiple choice (enable Allow multiple selections)

Options (example ‚Äî must match supplier form wording):
Venue
Catering
Photography / Videography
Floristry
Entertainment / Music
Styling / Decor
Lighting / AV
Staffing / Security
Planner / Coordinator
Other (please specify)

Add individual budget questions (one question per supplier). Example:

Q: Venue ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)

Type: Number (or Short text with numeric validation)

Required: optional (your choice)

Repeat for:

Catering ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)

Photography ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)

Floristry ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)

Entertainment ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)

Styling / Decor ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)

Lighting / AV ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)

Staffing ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)

Planner / Coordinator ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)

B ‚Äî Add the show-if logic for each budget question
In the form editor, click the Logic tab at the top (‚ö° icon).

If you don‚Äôt see the Logic tab, click the budget question itself and look for ‚ÄúAdd logic‚Äù or the three-dot menu (‚ãØ) ‚Üí Logic.

Choose Add rule (or Add a question rule / Question logic).

You want a rule that shows the budget question only if the multi-select contains the corresponding supplier.

Create a rule for each budget question. Use this exact format when you create each rule:

SHOW [Budget question] IF [What type(s) of suppliers are you looking for?] CONTAINS "Venue"
Then:

SHOW [Catering ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)] IF [What type(s) of suppliers are you looking for?] CONTAINS "Catering"
And so on for each supplier:

SHOW [Photography ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)] IF [What type(s) of suppliers are you looking for?] CONTAINS "Photography / Videography"

SHOW [Floristry ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)] IF [What type(s) of suppliers are you looking for?] CONTAINS "Floristry"

SHOW [Entertainment ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)] IF [What type(s) of suppliers are you looking for?] CONTAINS "Entertainment / Music"

SHOW [Styling / Decor ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)] IF [What type(s) of suppliers are you looking for?] CONTAINS "Styling / Decor"

SHOW [Lighting / AV ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)] IF [What type(s) of suppliers are you looking for?] CONTAINS "Lighting / AV"

SHOW [Staffing ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)] IF [What type(s) of suppliers are you looking for?] CONTAINS "Staffing / Security"

SHOW [Planner / Coordinator ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)] IF [What type(s) of suppliers are you looking for?] CONTAINS "Planner / Coordinator"

Tip: copy/paste these exact strings when creating the rules so the option text matches exactly. Logic matches by the option text.

Save each rule. You should see a small logic indicator next to the budget questions after saving.

C ‚Äî Confirm the flow (what happens)
If the respondent selects only Venue, they will be shown only the Venue budget question next.

If they select Venue + Catering, they will be shown Venue budget, then Catering budget, then proceed to the next section.

The form automatically skips any budget questions whose show-rule is false.

D ‚Äî Test it in Preview
Click Preview (eye icon).

Select different combinations:

Single pick (e.g., Venue) ‚Üí confirm only Venue budget appears.

Multiple picks (Venue + Catering + Photographer) ‚Üí confirm all three budget questions appear in sequence.

Submit a test entry and check the Responses tab (and your connected Google Sheet) to ensure each budget lives in its own column.

E ‚Äî Google Sheet mapping (what it looks like)
When you connect to Google Sheets, Typeform will create columns like:

What type(s) of suppliers are you looking for? (contains comma-separated choices)

Venue ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)

Catering ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)

Photography ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)

...etc.

Rows for budgets that weren‚Äôt shown will be blank ‚Äî that‚Äôs expected. Your matching logic later will look for non-blank budget fields corresponding to a client‚Äôs selected suppliers.

Troubleshooting & tips
No Logic/Question Rules option visible? ‚Äî Click the budget question itself and look for a small ‚Äúlogic‚Äù link or the three-dot menu (‚ãØ). If still not visible, ensure you have at least one multi-select question present and refresh the editor. Some very old Typeform UIs hide logic under question settings.

Make sure option text matches exactly between the supplier multi-select and the logic rules ‚Äî capitalization and punctuation matter.

Keep budgets numeric: use Number question type if available. If you must use Short text, tell users ‚Äúenter numbers only‚Äù and later clean in Sheets.

If you‚Äôre on a paid plan, you can also hide entire sections based on answers ‚Äî same principle, just apply rules to Section (Statement) cards.

Quick copy-paste checklist for you
1. Create multi-select Q: "What type(s) of suppliers are you looking for?" (allow multiple)
   Options: Venue | Catering | Photography / Videography | Floristry | Entertainment / Music | Styling / Decor | Lighting / AV | Staffing / Security | Planner / Coordinator | Other

2. Add budget questions:
   - "Venue ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)" (Number)
   - "Catering ‚Äî approximate budget (¬£)" (Number)
   - ...repeat for each supplier type...

3. For each budget question add rule:
   SHOW [Budget question] IF [What type(s) of suppliers are you looking for?] CONTAINS "[Exact option text]"

4. Preview form: choose multiple suppliers and confirm multiple budget questions display.

5. Test submission and check Google Sheets columns.

Perfect ‚Äî you‚Äôre so close! The issue is that Typeform logic jumps only handle one route per respondent at a time, and ‚ÄúMultiple choice (multiple selection)‚Äù questions don‚Äôt natively support branching to multiple follow-ups in a single pass.

Here‚Äôs what‚Äôs happening:

When someone selects multiple supplier types, Typeform doesn‚Äôt ‚Äúloop‚Äù through each one ‚Äî it picks just one logic route, usually the first that matches.

That‚Äôs why you‚Äôre still seeing all the budget questions appear ‚Äî because they‚Äôre not being conditionally hidden.

Let‚Äôs fix this properly üëá

üß≠ OPTION 1: Stay in Typeform (best for free / simple setup)
You‚Äôll want to:

Remove all logic jumps from Q3 (supplier selection).

Show all budget questions, but make each conditional on the supplier being selected.

Here‚Äôs how:

Step-by-step:
Click on Logic ‚Üí Question branching.

Add rules that look like this:

IF ‚ÄúWhat type of supplier(s) are you looking for?‚Äù includes ‚ÄúVenue‚Äù
THEN show ‚ÄúWhat is your approximate budget for your venue?‚Äù
Repeat this pattern for every supplier option:

IF includes ‚ÄúCatering‚Äù ‚Üí show ‚ÄúBudget for catering‚Äù
IF includes ‚ÄúPhotography / Videography‚Äù ‚Üí show ‚ÄúBudget for photography/videography‚Äù
...
Finally, make sure the form continues automatically to the next section after all shown budget questions (so you don‚Äôt end up with blanks showing for unselected suppliers).

‚úÖ Result:
Only the relevant budget questions appear depending on what supplier types they ticked.

üßÆ OPTION 2: (If you want perfect dynamic logic later)
If you upgrade to Typeform Plus or above, or connect with Tally, Formstack, or Google Forms + Glide, you can use conditional sections or branching loops to dynamically repeat the same question type (budget) per selected supplier.

That would let you say:

‚ÄúYou selected Catering and Floristry ‚Äî please enter your budget for each.‚Äù

And it would cycle through them automatically. But that‚Äôs not supported in free Typeform.

üß© So ‚Äî in your case:
Keep your multiple choice question as-is and:

Delete the existing ‚ÄúGo to‚Äù logic per supplier.

Replace with ‚ÄúShow/Hide‚Äù rules that display only the relevant budget questions.

Then ‚ÄúJump to next section‚Äù (S2: Location & Date).

üß≠ HOW TO ADD SHOW / HIDE LOGIC IN TYPEFORM
ü™Ñ Step 1: Open Logic Settings
Open your Typeform in Edit mode.

On the top toolbar, click ‚ÄúLogic‚Äù ‚Üí choose ‚ÄúQuestion branching‚Äù (not ‚ÄúJump to‚Äù or ‚ÄúScore‚Äù).

You should now see your question list on the left and logic rules panel on the right.

ü™Ñ Step 2: Select the Question
Click on your supplier selection question ‚Äî e.g.
‚ÄúWhat type of supplier(s) are you looking for?‚Äù

On the right-hand panel, click ‚ÄúAdd a rule‚Äù.

ü™Ñ Step 3: Build a ‚ÄúShow‚Äù Rule
Now you‚Äôll set a rule like this:

IF ‚Üí ‚ÄúWhat type of supplier(s) are you looking for?‚Äù ‚Üí includes ‚Üí ‚ÄúVenue‚Äù

THEN ‚Üí show ‚Üí ‚ÄúWhat is your approximate budget for your venue?‚Äù

‚úÖ You‚Äôll notice ‚Äúshow‚Äù is an option in the dropdown ‚Äî if you only see ‚Äúgo to,‚Äù scroll down in the dropdown until you see ‚Äúshow‚Äù.

ü™Ñ Step 4: Repeat for Each Supplier Type
Add one rule for each supplier option:

Supplier Type	Action
Venue	Show ‚ÄúWhat is your approximate budget for your venue?‚Äù
Catering	Show ‚ÄúWhat is your approximate budget for catering?‚Äù
Photography/Videography	Show ‚ÄúWhat is your approximate budget for photography/videography?‚Äù
Floristry	Show ‚ÄúWhat is your approximate budget for floristry?‚Äù
Entertainment/Music	Show ‚ÄúWhat is your approximate budget for entertainment/music?‚Äù
Styling/Decor	Show ‚ÄúWhat is your approximate budget for styling/decor?‚Äù
Lighting/AV	Show ‚ÄúWhat is your approximate budget for lighting/AV?‚Äù
Staffing/Security	Show ‚ÄúWhat is your approximate budget for staffing/security?‚Äù
Planner/Coordinator	Show ‚ÄúWhat is your approximate budget for a planner/coordinator?‚Äù
Other	Show ‚ÄúPlease specify supplier type‚Äù
ü™Ñ Step 5: Set End of Section Logic
After those rules, add one more at the bottom:

All other cases ‚Üí Jump to ‚Üí Next section (S2: Location & Date)

That makes sure people aren‚Äôt stuck seeing all the budget questions.

üîß To add validation to a question in Typeform:
Click on the question you want to edit (e.g. postcode, budget amount, etc.).

On the right-hand panel, scroll down until you see a section called ‚ÄúQuestion settings‚Äù or a small ‚öôÔ∏è cogwheel icon.

Click ‚ÄúAdd validation‚Äù (or it may show as ‚ÄúResponse validation‚Äù depending on your plan/version).

Choose from available validation types ‚Äî e.g.

Number ‚Üí ensures the answer is numeric (useful for budgets, distances, etc.)

Email ‚Üí ensures the answer includes @

URL ‚Üí for website or portfolio links

Text length ‚Üí for short answers

Regular expression (RegEx) ‚Üí for postcodes (Pro feature)

‚ú® Example uses:
Postcode field:
Validation ‚Üí Text + limit characters (or if Pro: add RegEx ^[A-Z]{1,2}\d[A-Z\d]? ?\d[A-Z]{2}$ for full postcodes)

Budget / Radius / Distance fields:
Validation ‚Üí Number only

Email field:
Typeform automatically validates when you set the field type to Email (no need to add separately).

üí° Tip: If you‚Äôre on a free plan, you won‚Äôt see the full validation menu ‚Äî only basic options (e.g. required/not required). The advanced validation (like postcode RegEx or numeric constraints) becomes available on Typeform Plus and Pro plans.

You said:
can I just do max characters 4 for the postcode question?
ChatGPT said:
You can, but that wouldn‚Äôt really work well for UK postcodes ‚Äî they‚Äôre too varied in length. Here‚Äôs why üëá

UK postcodes can range from 5 to 8 characters, depending on the area:

e.g. BA1 2PX (7 characters including space)

SW1A 1AA (7 characters)

W1A 0AX (6 characters)

EC1A 1BB (7 characters)

L1 8JQ (6 characters)

M1 1AE (5 characters)

So if you cap it at 4, almost no one will be able to enter their postcode correctly.

‚úÖ Better options:

Set max characters to 8 (and maybe min to 5)
That way, they can enter any valid postcode format.

Add a short note or placeholder, e.g.

Please enter your full postcode (e.g. BA1 2PX)

If you‚Äôre on a Pro plan, you can do it properly with postcode validation (using a regular expression).
But for now, the 5‚Äì8 character range plus that instruction is your best option.

ChatGPT said:
Thanks ‚Äî that makes it clear. I‚Äôll explain each question (Q8‚ÄìQ10) in plain language, why it matters, how suppliers should answer, how you should validate the answers, and how you‚Äôll use the data when matching to clients.

Q8 ‚Äî ‚ÄúWhat is your typical project or booking minimum?‚Äù
Plain meaning: the smallest fee you‚Äôll accept for a job/booking.
Why you ask it: some suppliers won‚Äôt take tiny, one-off jobs (e.g., a production company may not accept anything under ¬£2,000). This saves you sending irrelevant leads and saves suppliers time.

How suppliers should answer:

A single number in pounds (e.g. 500, 2500).

If they‚Äôre flexible, they can write 0 or negotiable (but numeric is easier to use).

Validation (Typeform):

Use the Number field or Short text with numeric validation.

Minimum value: 0. (Optional: set a sensible max e.g. 100000.)

How you‚Äôll use it in matching:

Filter client leads where the client‚Äôs budget per supplier ‚â• supplier minimum.

If client budget < supplier minimum, don‚Äôt send that lead (or mark as lower priority).

Example:
Supplier: booking minimum ¬£1,000
Client: photographer budget ¬£800 ‚Üí don‚Äôt match (or flag as low fit)

Q9 ‚Äî ‚ÄúWhat is your average event budget range?‚Äù
Plain meaning: the typical total spend range the supplier usually works within for an event.
Why you ask it: gives context about the supplier‚Äôs usual market ‚Äî luxury suppliers will usually choose higher ranges; budget suppliers will choose lower. It complements Q8 (minimum) by showing normal work scale.

How suppliers should answer:

Pick one of the ranges you provided (e.g. ¬£1,000‚Äì¬£3,000). This is not a single-number field ‚Äî it‚Äôs a category.

Validation (Typeform):

Multiple choice (force one answer). No need for number validation.

How you‚Äôll use it in matching:

Use it as a soft filter ‚Äî prefer suppliers whose average range aligns with the client‚Äôs total budget bracket.

Useful for prioritisation: if a client‚Äôs overall event budget is ¬£5k‚Äì¬£10k, you‚Äôll surface suppliers typically working in that range first.

Example:
Supplier: average range ¬£5k‚Äì¬£10k
Client: overall event budget ¬£1k‚Äì¬£5k ‚Üí supplier may be a stretch; push them lower in the match results.

Q10 ‚Äî ‚ÄúHow much advance notice do you typically require for bookings?‚Äù
Plain meaning: how far in advance the supplier needs to be booked.
Why you ask it: matches urgency ‚Äî a supplier requiring 3+ months‚Äô notice won‚Äôt be appropriate for a client needing something in 2 weeks.

How suppliers should answer:

Select the nearest option (e.g. 1‚Äì4 weeks, 1‚Äì3 months).

Validation (Typeform):

Multiple choice (required).

How you‚Äôll use it in matching:

Compare supplier lead time with a client‚Äôs event date. If a client‚Äôs event is in 2 weeks and a supplier needs 1‚Äì3 months, don‚Äôt match (or mark as low fit).

How Q8 + Q9 work together (quick summary)
Q8 (minimum) = hard constraint (supplier won‚Äôt take jobs below this).

Q9 (average range) = soft preference/fit (what they usually work on).
Use both for better matches: first filter by Q8 vs client budgets, then rank by Q9 alignment.

ChatGPT said:
