Here‚Äôs a clean, copy-and-paste-ready version you can drop straight into Google Docs ‚Äî I‚Äôve structured it so it‚Äôs concise, strategic, and actionable.

Fortunate Events: Business Model Outline
Overview
Fortunate Events is a curated matchmaking platform connecting clients (brands, couples, companies, individuals) planning events with suppliers (venues, caterers, florists, entertainment, stylists, etc.) based on budget, location, and style.

Initially, the model focuses on data collection and matching ‚Äî gathering and organising detailed supplier and client data to enable precise, efficient connections.
Once traction and volume are established, the business will evolve into a hybrid model, combining data-driven automation with premium, hands-on event curation.

Phase 1: Data Scraping & Matchmaking Model
Goal
Build a valuable dataset and network of verified event suppliers and client leads to establish the foundation for scalable matching and monetisation.

Key Components
Client Form: Captures event details ‚Äî type, size, location, budget, date, and supplier needs.

Supplier Form: Captures service details ‚Äî type of service, area covered, price range, availability, and capacity.

Database (Google Sheets / Airtable): Centralised system to store and filter client and supplier data for easy manual matching.

Process
Collect Data

Drive traffic to both forms via social media, targeted outreach, and partnerships.

Store all form responses automatically in a master database.

Manual Matching

Match suppliers and clients using key data points: event type, location, and budget.

Send curated introductions via email (using templates).

Track Engagement

Monitor which suppliers respond, convert, or request more leads.

Begin ranking suppliers by responsiveness and quality feedback.

Revenue Streams (Phase 1)
Lead Introduction Fees ‚Äì Suppliers pay a small fee per qualified lead or confirmed booking.

Featured Listings ‚Äì Suppliers can pay for priority visibility in future matchmaking rounds or public listings.

Data Insights / Reports ‚Äì Aggregated data can be used to provide insights into trends (e.g. ‚ÄúAverage spend on weddings in South West England‚Äù).

Advantages
Fast setup with minimal tech build.

Generates immediate supplier and client interest.

Builds a data asset that fuels Phase 2 growth.

Goal

Key Components

Free basic profile (limited visibility).

Commission on Confirmed Bookings

Here‚Äôs your Lumo22 business model, rewritten cleanly so you can copy-paste straight into a Google Doc. It reflects the data-scraping-first approach, with the flexibility to evolve into the hybrid model later.

Lumo22: Business Model Overview
1. Concept Summary
Lumo22 is an intelligent event-supplier matchmaking platform designed to connect verified event suppliers with active events already taking place.
The platform initially uses data scraping and aggregation to identify and categorise events, then matches them with suppliers based on relevance, proximity, and suitability.
As the user base grows, Lumo22 will evolve into a hybrid model, onboarding event organisers directly through forms and partnerships.

2. Phase 1: Data-Scraping Model
Objective
Generate a high-quality database of live event opportunities and use this to match suppliers automatically ‚Äî building proof of concept, traction, and supplier-side revenue before expanding to direct client onboarding.

Data Sources
Event listing platforms (e.g. Eventbrite, Skiddle, DesignMyNight, local council listings)

Corporate event calendars and conference directories

Festival and wedding directories

Local news, social media event pages, and ticketing sites

Data Captured
Event name, date, and location (first half of postcode)

Event type (wedding, festival, corporate, etc.)

Estimated audience size

Venue name (if applicable)

Organiser name / company / contact details

Ticket price range (proxy for budget)

3. Matching Process
Lumo22 will:

Categorise each event by type, size, and region.

Cross-reference with supplier database (location, radius, category, budget range).

Flag suitable opportunities and notify suppliers automatically.

Optionally, allow suppliers to ‚Äúbid‚Äù or express interest in matched events via the platform or email notification.

4. Supplier Database
Information Captured
Supplier type and subcategory (e.g., florist, caterer, lighting, entertainment)

Service area (postcode prefix + travel radius)

Average project budget range

Minimum booking value

Portfolio link / social media

Availability and lead time

Public liability insurance status

Onboarding
Suppliers register via Typeform (or Lumo22 landing page) and are verified manually or through automated checks (social links, website validation).

5. Revenue Model
Phase 1 (Scraping model)
Subscription fee: Monthly or annual listing for suppliers (e.g. ¬£25/month or ¬£250/year)

Pay-per-lead: Suppliers pay per verified opportunity (e.g. ¬£5‚Äì10 per match)

Introductory offer: Free 30-day trial or discounted onboarding for early adopters

Phase 2 (Hybrid model)
Client acquisition: Event organisers directly submit requests through Lumo22

Commission model: % fee (e.g. 5‚Äì10%) on confirmed bookings

Premium supplier tier: Enhanced listings, early access to leads, or featured placement

6. Value Proposition
For Suppliers
Save hours of outreach ‚Äî matched only with relevant, verified events.

Get real opportunities based on data-driven leads.

Flexible pricing (subscription or per-lead).

Build credibility via a vetted supplier network.

For Organisers (Phase 2)
Access a curated directory of vetted, high-quality suppliers.

Streamlined matching process ‚Äî one form, multiple quotes.

Reduced time spent sourcing and verifying suppliers.

7. Technical Setup
Phase 1 (Scraping + Forms)
Typeform for supplier onboarding and structured data collection.

Web scraping scripts (Python / Zapier integrations / Airtable automations).

Airtable or Notion database for event + supplier management.

Simple website / landing page (Notion or Webflow).

MailerLite or ConvertKit for notifications and onboarding comms.

Phase 2 (Hybrid platform)
Custom platform with two portals (organisers + suppliers).

Integrated matching algorithm and user dashboard.

Stripe integration for payment processing.

Email automation and CRM integration.

8. Next Steps
Immediate (this week):

Finalise supplier Typeform and connect it to Airtable.

Define scraping parameters and test manual data import.

Design notification workflow for supplier matches.

Next phase (in 4‚Äì6 weeks):

Begin data scraping and validation.

Onboard initial suppliers (10‚Äì20 beta testers).

Build landing page with lead capture and email automation.

‚öôÔ∏è Option 1: Direct Typeform ‚Üí Airtable Integration (Best option if you have Pro Typeform or Zapier)

‚úÖ What happens:

Typeform automatically detects your form questions.

It either connects to an existing Airtable base or creates a new one with matching fields.

Every new form submission becomes a new record in Airtable.

üß© How to set it up:

In Typeform, open your Supplier Registration form.

Go to Connect (top menu).

Search Airtable in the integrations list.

Click Connect ‚Üí sign in to Airtable.

Choose Create a new base (or pick your existing ‚ÄúLumo22 Supplier & Event Database‚Äù).

Match each Typeform question to a field (Typeform often does this automatically).

Click Save & Activate.

Now, when someone submits your form, you‚Äôll see the data appear instantly in Airtable üéØ

‚öôÔ∏è Option 2: Using Zapier (if Typeform‚Äôs native Airtable integration isn‚Äôt available on your plan)

Steps:

Create a free Zapier account.

Choose:

Trigger: ‚ÄúNew Typeform response‚Äù

Action: ‚ÄúCreate record in Airtable‚Äù

Connect both accounts.

Map your form fields (Typeform ‚Üí Airtable columns).

Test it once ‚Äî and you‚Äôre done.

‚öñÔ∏è Option 1: Stick with Google Sheets + Zapier
‚úÖ Best for: Early-stage testing, low-cost setup, fast iteration.

üß© How it works:
Typeform already pushes new responses into Google Sheets automatically.

You then connect Zapier to pull that data wherever you need (CRM, email sequences, matching logic, etc.).

üí° Example workflow:
Typeform ‚Üí Google Sheet (already done ‚úÖ)

Zapier watches the sheet ‚Üí when a new row is added ‚Üí it triggers:

Matching logic (e.g. find suppliers in same region).

Email notification or lead alert.

Adds both client + supplier data to a CRM or Notion/Airtable later.

‚úÖ Pros:
Free or very cheap to start.

You already have it working (no rebuild needed).

Works perfectly for early testing and proof of concept.

Easy to move data later to Airtable or another CRM.

‚ùå Cons:
Sheets aren‚Äôt great for scaling ‚Äî they get messy as you grow.

No built-in ‚Äúrelational‚Äù structure (can‚Äôt easily link one client record to multiple suppliers).

Harder to visualize data for matching or analytics.

‚öñÔ∏è Option 2: Switch to Airtable
‚úÖ Best for: When you start doing structured data matching (clients ‚Üî suppliers).

üí° Example setup:
Airtable has two tables: ‚ÄúClients‚Äù and ‚ÄúSuppliers.‚Äù

Each supplier record includes region, category, budget range, etc.

Each client submission triggers a search or view that shows ‚Äúmatching suppliers.‚Äù

‚úÖ Pros:
Purpose-built for databases and relationships.

Easier to filter + match suppliers dynamically.

Can integrate directly with automation tools like Make (Integromat) later.

‚ùå Cons:
Takes longer to set up initially.

Direct Typeform ‚Üí Airtable integration might require a paid plan.

Steeper learning curve if you‚Äôve never worked with relational databases.

Output options	Exports to Google Sheets, JSON, CSV easily	Same + deeper integration possibilities, API access for more advanced pipelines
Learning curve	Lower entry barrier ‚Äî great for early stage	Higher entry barrier but gives more control and power later
Best fit	Start-up mode, fast deployment, basic scraping	Growth mode, high volume, lots of data sources & complex logic

You said:
when defining scraping targets can this just be set to nationwide rather than having to list the cities individually
ChatGPT said:
Yes ‚Äî absolutely, Sophie üëå

You can absolutely set your scraping target to nationwide instead of listing cities individually ‚Äî but here‚Äôs the nuance depending on how each platform structures its search pages:

üü¶ 1. Eventbrite (Recommended first source)
‚úÖ Yes, you can set to nationwide, but you‚Äôll get better quality results if you filter slightly.
Eventbrite‚Äôs nationwide URL looks like this:

https://www.eventbrite.co.uk/d/united-kingdom/all-events/
That will pull every event in the UK, but note:

It can generate tens of thousands of rows, many of which are online-only or irrelevant (like webinars, virtual courses, etc.).

The scraper may time out if it tries to handle too much data at once.

üí° Best compromise:
Use a small list of region-level searches (e.g., ‚ÄúSouth West‚Äù, ‚ÄúLondon‚Äù, ‚ÄúNorth West‚Äù) rather than city-by-city.

Region	Eventbrite URL

Things to watch for / common gotchas

Too broad searches produce noisy data ‚Äî prefer region/wedding-fair keyword combos.

Respect site robots/terms and GDPR ‚Äî you‚Äôre scraping public listings & business contact pages only. Don‚Äôt scrape private groups or personal info.

You said:
could I build my own scraper to use for free?
ChatGPT said:
Excellent question ‚Äî and yes, you absolutely can build your own scraper to use for free (or nearly free), depending on how technical you want to get and how ‚Äúhands-off‚Äù you want the automation to be.

Let‚Äôs look at the realistic routes üëá

üß© OPTION 1 ‚Äî DIY Scraper (Code-Based, Free)
Best for: Low-budget, flexible control, willing to do light coding setup
Tools:

Python + libraries: requests, BeautifulSoup, pandas, playwright (for sites with JavaScript)

Run locally on your laptop or in Google Colab (free cloud runtime).

Store results in a Google Sheet using the gspread library.

Example (for Eventbrite):

import requests, pandas as pd
from bs4 import BeautifulSoup

url = "https://www.eventbrite.co.uk/d/united-kingdom--england/events/"
r = requests.get(url)
soup = BeautifulSoup(r.text, "html.parser")

events = []
for e in soup.select("div.eds-event-card-content__content"):
    title = e.select_one("div.eds-is-hidden-accessible")
    date = e.select_one("div.eds-event-card-content__sub-title")
    link = e.find("a", href=True)
    if title and link:
        events.append({
            "Event Name": title.text.strip(),
            "Event Date": date.text.strip() if date else "",
            "URL": link["href"]
        })

df = pd.DataFrame(events)
df.to_csv("events.csv", index=False)
This takes about 10 minutes to set up, and you can run it anytime to refresh data.

üß± OPTION 2 ‚Äî Google Sheets + Apps Script Scraper
Best for: Non-coders, simple data needs, staying inside Google
Tools: Google Sheets + built-in Apps Script (free)
You can use UrlFetchApp.fetch() to grab a page and parse text or JSON, then fill rows automatically.
‚úÖ Pros: Runs on schedule (hourly, daily).
üö´ Cons: Doesn‚Äôt handle JavaScript-heavy sites like Eventbrite as easily.

‚öôÔ∏è OPTION 3 ‚Äî No-Code Tools (Hybrid Free Plan)
If you‚Äôd rather not code and want something point-and-click:

Browse AI (Free plan: 50 runs/month)

Bardeen (Free automation, Chrome extension)

Apify (free tier) (has 10‚Äì20 free credits/month)

You can train these to scrape specific fields on Eventbrite pages visually ‚Äî no programming.

üîÑ Recommendation for You (Sophie)
Since you‚Äôre aiming to test & automate lead collection quickly:

Start with Browse AI ‚Äî it has a free plan and an easy ‚Äúrecord once, run anytime‚Äù visual setup.

Then, later, migrate to your own Python script when you want control and no limits.

Would you like me to walk you through setting up Browse AI to pull UK event listings into your existing Google Sheet? It‚Äôs genuinely one of the easiest ‚Äúscrape without code‚Äù methods out there right now.

Great ‚Äî smart thinking. You‚Äôre right: individual wedding guest lists / private wedding details are private and off-limits, but there are a bunch of legal, high-value, and practical signals you can use from venue contracts, venue activity, and B2B sources to surface opportunities for Lumo22 without touching private personal data.

Below I‚Äôll give you a practical, legally safe playbook ‚Äî tactics you can use today and a prioritized roadmap (what to try first), plus templates you can copy-paste (venue outreach email, partnership pitch, and search keywords).

Useable signals & data sources (legal / practical)
These sources tell you where events are happening or where demand is being created ‚Äî and they‚Äôre B2B or public:

Venue event calendars / ‚Äúwhat‚Äôs on‚Äù pages

Many hotels, venues and conference centres publish public event listings and calendars. These are rich, legal event signals.

Wedding fairs & exhibitor lists

Organisers publish exhibitor lists and event dates ‚Äî great for finding active supplier networks and event organisers.

Venue ‚Äúbookings / availability‚Äù pages and hire rates

Venue pricing and availability pages indicate they host events and when they‚Äôre busy.

Venue & hotel press releases / news pages

Announcements about bookings, conferences, weddings, or refurbishments often name dates/organisers.

Venue/Hotel PMS & Event Management systems (Cvent, Venuewise, etc.) ‚Äî via partnerships / integrations

These are private but many vendors integrate/partner; you can propose integrations or referral deals.

Local council planning & licencing records

Temporary event notices, alcohol licencing, or public events permits are public records and can indicate upcoming events (varies by council).

Business-facing directories / booking platforms

Tagvenue, Venuefinder, Hitched, Bridebook, AddtoEvent ‚Äî these list venues, event types they host, and contact details.

Google Business Profiles / Maps + reviews

Venue FAQs and reviews often mention ‚Äúwe host weddings/conferences‚Äù ‚Äî searchable and scrappable via Places API (note API costs).

Social signals (Instagram, Facebook Pages)

Venue posts with ‚Äúwedding‚Äù, ‚Äúbooked‚Äù, ‚Äúevent‚Äù hashtags; public and useful for timing.

Supplier invoices / RFP portals (procurement)

Not public, but venues often use procurement portals for corporate events ‚Äî you can approach procurement teams and offer match services.

Tactics you can use (practical, safe, ranked)
Priority A ‚Äî Immediate, low-cost, high ROI
Scrape & monitor venue ‚Äúwhat‚Äôs on‚Äù / events pages

Target hotels, conference centres, and venues‚Äô public event calendars.

Why: direct signal of events and B2B organiser contacts.

Tools: Browse AI / Apify / custom script + Google Sheet.

Partner outreach to venues (referral / co-marketing)

Offer venues a simple benefit: vetted suppliers for their clients, a referral fee on successful matches, or free listings.

Why: venues often want vetted supplier lists to support clients; this gives you a warm pipeline.

I‚Äôll give an outreach template below.

Scrape exhibitor lists & wedding fair organiser pages

Extract exhibitor lists (suppliers) and organiser contact pages ‚Äî gold for supplier-side growth and discovering who‚Äôs actively marketing to couples.

Monitor venue social media for booking announcements

Use Instagram hashtag monitoring (e.g. venue name + #booked, #wedding, #conference) and Google Alerts for venue names + ‚Äúwedding‚Äù.

Why: fast indicator of recent bookings or seasonal demand.

Priority B ‚Äî Medium effort, good payoff
Use council public records (event/TENs/licences)

Many councils publish temporary event notices and premises licence applications. These indicate planned public events.

Caveat: formats vary by council; some require manual checking or scrapers per council site.

Create a venue partnership package (dashboard / referral portal)

Offer venues a simple portal or spreadsheet where they can drop event briefs (date, type, brief). You can then match suppliers and split leads/revenue.

Leverage venue booking platforms & directories

Scrape or partner with Tagvenue, Venuefinder, Bridebook ‚Äî many list event suitability and contact emails.

Priority C ‚Äî Longer term / require agreements or investment
PMS / Event management integrations (Cvent, Venuebook, etc.)

Build an API/integration or partnership so venues push event briefs straight to Lumo22.

Requires negotiation and development but yields high-quality, repeatable leads.

Procurement & corporate event portals

For corporate events, approach procurement teams or event agencies with a ‚Äúsupplier matching‚Äù service.

Paid API approach (Google Places / Ticketing APIs)

Use Google Places API, Ticketmaster API etc. to get structured venue/event data (costly but reliable and scalable).

Legal & privacy checklist (must-do)
Only collect and use public business information (venue pages, directories, public permits).

Don‚Äôt scrape or store personal data of private individuals (no wedding guest lists, private Facebook group content, or closed pages).

If you store contact emails for outreach, ensure you have a lawful basis for contacting (legitimate interest or consent) and provide a clear opt-out.

Keep your privacy policy and supplier T&Cs clear about how you use data and how suppliers/venues can opt out.

Recurring potential (creators often do multiple campaigns).

Less competition than traditional lead-gen.

Next steps to test:

Build a short form for creator sign-ups (collect niche, audience size, platforms).

Reach out to 10 brands and 20 creators to test match value and price.

2. Specialty Home Service Lead Portal (Luxury Renovation/Domestic Projects)
Market size & trend:

The U.S. home improvement market is ~$700 billion per year.

Luxury renovation segments (smart home, outdoor kitchens, high-end landscaping) are fast-growing.

What you would collect/scrape:

Homeowners expressing interest in a project (via forums, Facebook Marketplace, Houzz posts, planning permits).

Service providers: luxury contractors, architects, high-end landscapers, smart home integrators.

Location + budget data, project type.

Revenue model:

Lead fee per qualified project (e.g., $100-$300 per homeowner-to-contractor lead).

Subscription tier for contractors (premium access to leads, exports).

High budget projects ‚Üí contractors willing to pay more for good leads.

Less fragmentation in premium segment.

You said:
Is oswald a good font to use to pair with gotham/poppins?
ChatGPT said:
Good question ‚Äî yes, Oswald can work very well depending on how you use it, but there are a few nuances üëá

üß© Font pairing notes for Oswald + Gotham/Poppins
Font	Style	Use Case	Notes
Oswald	Condensed sans-serif	Headlines, subheads, accent text	Modern and impactful. Its tall, narrow letters create contrast against rounder fonts like Gotham or Poppins.
Gotham	Geometric sans-serif	Logos, headers, CTAs	Clean and professional ‚Äî great for your brand base.
Poppins	Geometric sans-serif (Google Font)	Body text or secondary headlines	Softer and more approachable than Gotham. Balances Oswald‚Äôs rigidity nicely.
‚úÖ When it works best
Oswald (Headings) + Poppins (Body text) ‚Üí best free pairing that mirrors Gotham‚Äôs style.

Oswald (Accent text / small section headers) + Gotham (Logo) + Poppins (Paragraphs) ‚Üí gives a modern ‚Äúeditorial‚Äù feel ‚Äî bold but not harsh.

Keep Oswald at light or regular weight for clean contrast (avoid extra-bold unless for hero headers).

‚ö†Ô∏è When to avoid
If your layout is very condensed or narrow, Oswald‚Äôs vertical compression can feel tight.

Don‚Äôt mix Oswald and Gotham together in long passages ‚Äî use Oswald sparingly for contrast.

üé® Example setup for your Lumo22 site
Element	Font	Size	Weight	Colour
Logo	Gotham	N/A	Bold	#FFFFFF
Main Headings	Oswald	42‚Äì48px	Regular / Semi-bold	#FFFFFF
Body Text	Poppins	16‚Äì18px	Regular	#EAEAEA
Buttons	Poppins	16px	600 (Semi-bold)	#212122 on #FFD748
üëâ Verdict:
Yes ‚Äî Oswald + Poppins (with your logo in Gotham) is an excellent, free, and cohesive combination.
It keeps your brand professional but still bold and easy to read across web and mobile.

Would you like me to show you two visual examples (layout mockup styles) of how those fonts could be applied together for your ‚ÄúComing Soon‚Äù page design?

You said:
What do you recommend I should use for branding fonts- final verdict
ChatGPT said:
Perfect ‚Äî let‚Äôs lock this in properly. Based on your goals (modern, professional, scalable brand for Lumo22) and the tools you‚Äôre using (Carrd free plan), here‚Äôs the final verdict for your brand font system üëá

üíõ Lumo22 Brand Font System ‚Äî Final Recommendation
Primary Font: Poppins
Use for: Body text, secondary headlines, and buttons.

Why: It‚Äôs clean, geometric, and freely available on Carrd + Google Fonts. It visually aligns with Gotham‚Äôs structure while being more versatile for web use.

Weights to use:

Regular (400) for body

Semi-bold (600) for subheadings and CTAs

Bold (700) for short headers if Oswald isn‚Äôt used

Accent Font: Oswald
Use for: Main headers, short statements, or section titles.

Why: Adds impact and distinction, perfect for the strong, confident tone of an outdoor home design / innovation brand.

Weights to use: Regular (400) or Medium (500) ‚Äî avoid extra bold for elegance.

Tip: Use uppercase Oswald for short, statement headers (e.g. ‚ÄúBUILD BETTER OUTDOORS‚Äù).

Logo Font: Gotham
Use for: The Lumo22 logo only.

Why: It‚Äôs premium and gives your brand authority. Keeping it logo-only makes it timeless and scalable if you move off Carrd later.

Fallback: If you ever need a web-safe match, use Poppins Semi-Bold as your Gotham substitute online.

‚ú® How to Apply This
Element	Font	Style	Colour
Logo	Gotham Bold	Uppercase	White (#FFFFFF)
Headings	Oswald Regular	Uppercase / 42‚Äì48px	White (#FFFFFF)
Body text	Poppins Regular	Sentence case / 16‚Äì18px	#EAEAEA
Accent text / CTAs	Poppins Semi-Bold	Uppercase	Yellow (#FFD748)
üß† Quick Style Summary
Modern, minimal, bold.

Contrast between the structured (Oswald) and the approachable (Poppins).

Easy to implement across Carrd, Canva, and social templates.

Future-proof if you expand to a full site later.

‚úÖ Final Verdict:

Logo: Gotham
Headings: Oswald
Body: Poppins

C ‚Äî Conditional Logic & Tips (Typeform logic)
Contractor form logic:

If ‚ÄúAre you taking new clients?‚Äù = No ‚Üí show a short message (you‚Äôll store this as ‚ÄúNot taking clients‚Äù) and ask if they want to be notified later (Yes/No). Then end form.

If Consent = No ‚Üí end with message: cannot process.

Homeowner form logic:

If Project types = Other ‚Üí show short text to describe

If Consent = No ‚Üí show message and end.

D ‚Äî Typeform settings recommendations
Language & Branding: Add Lumo22 logo at top (image) and set theme colors:

Accent: #FFD748 (button)

Background: #212122 (or keep default white; Typeform‚Äôs free theming is limited)

Required fields: Set all emails and consent as required.

Notifications: Turn on email notifications to hello@lumo22.com for every new response.

Collect respondent emails: Already via Email field.

Response limits: Free Typeform allows unlimited responses but file uploads and logic might require a paid plan for advanced features.

E ‚Äî Connect Typeform ‚Üí Google Sheets (native) ‚Äî quick steps
Open your Typeform ‚Üí go to Connect tab.

Find Google Sheets integration ‚Üí click Connect.

Choose the Google account and the specific spreadsheet (or create a new one).

Map Typeform questions to sheet columns (use the mappings I gave above).

Save and test with one submission.

If Typeform native integration throws errors, the fallback is Zapier:

Trigger: Typeform ‚Üí New Entry

Action: Google Sheets ‚Üí Create Spreadsheet Row

Map fields and test.

ChatGPT said:
Nice ‚Äî let‚Äôs build the database schema + scraping structure for Lumo22 (Outdoor Living). I‚Äôll give you everything you can copy-paste into Google Sheets / Airtable and into your scraper setup. This is a production-ready blueprint: fields, validation, enrichment, scraping targets, scheduling, deduplication, matching logic (postcode/ZIP radius), revenue-share tracking, automation ideas, and a short rollout plan.

1) Overview ‚Äî what we‚Äôre building
Two core tables: Contractors and Leads.

A few supporting tables/views: Matches, Deals, Waitlist, Sources.

Scrapers pull contractor data from Google Maps / Yelp / Houzz / Thumbtack / Instagram directory pages into the Contractors table.

Leads come from paid ads, landing page Typeform, or future scrapers (forums/requests).

Matching uses geographic (lat/lon + radius), service type, budget, and capacity.

Track revenue-share and per-lead sales in Deals.

2) Core schema ‚Äî copy-paste headers
Contractors table (sheet name: Contractors)
contractor_id, date_added, source, source_url, business_name, contact_name, email, phone, website, instagram, address_full, city, state, zip, lat, lon, services, service_tags, typical_project_min, typical_project_max, avg_project_value, capacity_per_month, taking_new_clients, revenue_model_pref, price_per_lead_usd, revenue_share_percent, rating, reviews_count, verified (yes/no), notes, status
contractor_id = unique ID (e.g., C0001).

services = comma list (e.g. "Decking,Outdoor Kitchen,Lighting").

service_tags = normalized tags for matching (lowercase, hyphenated).

typical_project_min/max = numbers in USD.

capacity_per_month = numeric.

taking_new_clients = yes/no.

revenue_model_pref = "per-lead", "revenue-share", "both".

verified = whether you manually checked them.

status = New / Contacted / Trial / Paying / Paused.

Leads table (sheet name: Leads)
lead_id, date_submitted, source, submission_url, first_name, last_name, email, phone, address_full, city, state, zip, lat, lon, project_types, project_tags, budget_range, budget_min, budget_max, urgency, timeline, description, images_link, match_status, matched_contractor_ids, notes
budget_range options: "Under $2k","$2k‚Äì$10k","$10k‚Äì$30k","$30k+".

match_status: New / Matched / Contacted / Accepted / Closed / Unqualified.

matched_contractor_ids: list of contractor_id(s) you sent the lead to.

Matches table (automatic view)
match_id, lead_id, contractor_id, date_matched, distance_miles, score, status, sent_date, contractor_response, deal_id
score = composite match score (0‚Äì100) from rules (see Matching logic).

status = Sent / Interested / Rejected / Booked.

Deals table (for revenue tracking)
deal_id, match_id, lead_id, contractor_id, date_booked, deal_value_usd, commission_percent, commission_amount_usd, payout_status, notes
3) Data validation rules (quick)
email column: validate with regex ^[^\s@]+@[^\s@]+\.[^\s@]+$ (Sheets custom or Apps Script).

zip (US): 5-digit regex ^\d{5}$ or ZIP+4 ^\d{5}(-\d{4})?$.

phone: keep E.164 preferred; normalize later via lib or service.

lat/lon: numeric; lat between -90 and 90, lon between -180 and 180.

4) How to get lat/lon
Preferred: have the scraper return lat/lon (Google Maps / Yelp records often include them).

Fallback: use a Geocoding API (Google Geocoding, OpenCage, LocationIQ) to batch convert address_full or zip ‚Üí lat/lon.

In Google Sheets: you can use Apps Script to call a geocode API and fill lat/lon.

5) Scraping targets & inputs (prioritised)
Start with these sources in this order (highest ROI first):

Google Maps (search: ‚Äúlandscaper near {city}‚Äù, ‚Äúdeck builder {city}‚Äù, etc.) ‚Äî best coverage + contact info.

Yelp ‚Äî business pages, phone, website, categories.

Houzz ‚Äî great for portfolios and higher-end contractors.

Thumbtack / HomeAdvisor ‚Äî leads directories (may block heavy scraping).

Instagram ‚Äî for portfolio + contact email or DM. Scrape business profile meta.

Local directories / Chamber pages / Angie's List.

6) Scraper configuration (Apify / Browse AI / custom)
Type: Google Maps Scraper actor or ‚ÄúWebsite Crawler‚Äù.

Input: search queries & location (nationwide or list of target cities). Example query list:

"deck builder {city} {state}", "landscaping company {city} {state}", "outdoor kitchen installer {city} {state}", "pergola builder {city} {state}"
Fields to extract: business name, business URL, phone, email (if available), address, city, state, zip, lat, lon, categories, rating, reviews_count, opening_hours, image_urls.

Pagination: max pages per search 5‚Äì10 initially; set maxResults to 100 per query.

Throttling & rate limits: obey target site's robots; run at low concurrency to avoid bans. Use proxy pools if scaling.

7) Scheduling & cadence
Initial run: scrape seed cities (2‚Äì3) with high homeowner density.

Recurring schedule: run scrapers weekly for freshness (or daily for high-volume cities once validated).

Incremental runs: keep date_added and only append new records; flag changed records for re-check.

8) Deduplication & enrichment process
Dedupe rules (run after each scrape):

Normalize business_name (lowercase, strip punctuation) + zip ‚Äî if same, treat as duplicate.

If website URLs match ‚Üí unify.

If phone numbers match ‚Üí unify.

Enrichment steps:

Use email-finder APIs (Hunter, Snov.io, Apollo) to get contact emails for businesses missing them.

Use a small script to fetch website and search for mailto: links if API fails.

Pull social links (Instagram) for portfolio verification.

Add rating and reviews_count from source.

9) Matching logic (priority and scoring)
Match a lead to contractors using a composite score (max 100). Example scoring weights:

Service match (required): if contractor offers any of lead's project_tags ‚Üí +40 points (required else score=0).

Geographic proximity: calculate distance (miles) between lead and contractor lat/lon:

<=10 miles = +30

10‚Äì25 miles = +20

25‚Äì50 miles = +10

50 miles = 0

Budget fit: contractor typical_project_min/max overlaps lead budget range:

Good fit = +20

Partial fit = +10

No fit = 0

Capacity: if contractor capacity_per_month > 0 add +10; else +0

Verified + rating: verified = +5, rating >4.5 = +5

Total = sum (cap at 100). Choose top 3 contractors by score > threshold (e.g., >=50) and send lead.

10) Distance calculation in Google Sheets
If you have lat/lon, use the Haversine formula in Sheets:

=LET(
  lat1, RADIANS(lead_lat),
  lon1, RADIANS(lead_lon),
  lat2, RADIANS(contractor_lat),
  lon2, RADIANS(contractor_lon),
  dlat, lat2-lat1,
  dlon, lon2-lon1,
  a, SIN(dlat/2)^2 + COS(lat1)*COS(lat2)*SIN(dlon/2)^2,
  c, 2*ATAN2(SQRT(a), SQRT(1-a)),
  3958.8 * c
)
Replace lead_lat etc. with cell refs. 3958.8 miles Earth radius.

If Sheets version doesn't support LET, use long formula widely available.

11) Automation flows (Zapier / Make / Native)
Basic flow (scraper ‚Üí match ‚Üí notify contractor):

Scraper exports to Contractors sheet (or Airtable).

New contractor rows create Contractor records.

When a new Lead row appears (from Typeform or ad), trigger a workflow:

Calculate candidate contractors via script (match by tags + distance + budget).

Compose email to top N contractors with lead details (include brief & link to lead row).

Update Matches table with sent_date and contractor_id.

Contractor replies ‚Üí update Matches.status. If contractor reports Booked ‚Üí create Deal record and compute commission.

Tools:

Use Zapier for simple automations (Typeform ‚Üí Sheets).

Use Make.com for conditional logic and batch tasks.

For heavier logic (matching, scoring), use a small Google Apps Script or a lightweight serverless function (Google Cloud Function, Vercel) that queries Sheets/Airtable API, computes matches and writes back.

12) Revenue-share tracking
When a contractor confirms a booking, record deal_value_usd in Deals table.

Calculate commission_amount_usd = deal_value_usd * commission_percent.

Send invoice/statement monthly to contractors (if using revenue-share) and track payout_status.

Suggested payment process:

Contractors invoice Lumo22 for their commission OR Lumo22 invoices contractor for leads sold. Choose simplest initial route: contractors pay you after a booked job (collect via Stripe invoice).

13) QA & monitoring
Weekly sanity checks: sample 10 random contractor records and verify contact info.

Monitor scraper success/failure logs; alert on >10% failure.

Keep status flags for manual review.

14) Privacy & legal notes (must-do)
Only store business/public contact info.

For emails scraped that are personal/private (e.g., personal gmail for business owner), ensure your outreach is business-related and provide an opt-out.

Maintain a clear privacy page describing your data use and a contact for deletion requests.

For revenue-share deals, use a simple written agreement before sending exclusive leads.

Day 0: Create Sheets/Airtable structure (copy headers above).
Day 1: Configure Apify / BrowseAI to scrape 3 seed cities (pick 2 metro areas). Export to Contractors.
Day 2: Run dedupe, enrichment (emails), geocode missing lat/lon.
Day 3: Create contractor outreach list; send 20 test outreach messages offering free trial leads.
Day 4: Create a small Facebook ad or landing page Typeform to generate 10 homeowner leads.
Day 5: Match leads ‚Üí send to contractors ‚Üí log responses.
Day 6: Iterate on scoring thresholds and pricing.

17) Example matching pseudo-code (for Apps Script / Node)
// Inputs: lead object (lat, lon, tags, budget_min, budget_max)
const candidates = contractors.filter(c => intersects(c.services, lead.tags));
candidates.forEach(c => {
  const dist = haversine(lead.lat, lead.lon, c.lat, c.lon);
  const geoScore = dist <= 10 ? 30 : dist <= 25 ? 20 : dist <= 50 ? 10 : 0;
  const budgetScore = overlapBudget(c.typical_min, c.typical_max, lead.budget_min, lead.budget_max) ? 20 : 10;
  const capacityScore = c.capacity_per_month > 0 ? 10 : 0;
  const verifiedScore = c.verified ? 5 : 0;
  const ratingScore = (c.rating>=4.5) ? 5 : 0;
  const total = 40 + geoScore + budgetScore + capacityScore + verifiedScore + ratingScore; // 40 = service match
  c.matchScore = total;
});
candidates.sort((a,b)=>b.matchScore-a.matchScore);
return candidates.slice(0,3);

Zapier / Make: free tier then $20+/month.

Google Sheets / Airtable: free tiers OK to start; Airtable more structured.

Email enrichment (Hunter, Snov.io): small monthly cost or pay-per-use.

PART C ‚Äî SCRAPING CONTRACTORS (Apify step-by-step)
Goal: populate the Contractors sheet with businesses (name, phone, website, address, zip, lat/lon if available).

A. Using Apify (recommended)

Sign in to Apify (apify.com).

In Apify, go to Store / Actors and search for Google Maps scraper or Yelp scraper. (Example actors: google-maps-scraper, newpo/eventbrite-scraper ‚Äî choose a Google Maps actor).

Create a small list of seed queries (start with 6‚Äì10). Example:

"landscaper Austin TX",
"deck builder Austin TX",
"outdoor kitchen installer Austin TX",
"landscaper Miami FL",
"deck builder Miami FL",
"pergola builder Miami FL"
Open the actor, click Run, and paste one query at a time into the input field (actors usually accept a JSON input like {"search":"landscaper austin tx","maxResults":100}) ‚Äî follow actor instructions.

For Fields to extract, ensure actor returns:

business_name, phone, website, address_full, city, state, zip (or postal code), lat, lon, categories, rating, reviews_count, source_url, image_urls

Run the actor and Export results: choose Google Sheets export (Apify supports Sheets integration) or CSV download. If CSV, upload to Contractors sheet (File > Import ‚Üí append to existing sheet).

Repeat for each query and for Yelp/Houzz as needed.

B. If you prefer no-code visual scraping (alternative)

Use Browse AI (visual scraper) to record extraction on a sample search results page; set outputs to Google Sheets. Similar workflow but slightly different UI.

Notes: start slow (100 results per query) to avoid blocks. Use Apify‚Äôs free credit then upgrade modestly.

PART E ‚Äî GEOCODING (fill lat/lon where missing)
You need lat/lon to compute distances. Use LocationIQ (cheap/free tier) or OpenCage.

Get API Key:

Sign up LocationIQ (locationiq.com) and copy API key.

Apps Script to geocode missing rows (do this in the Sheet):

In Google Sheets: Extensions > Apps Script.

Replace the default code with this script (paste and save). Update LOCATIONIQ_KEY with your key. Then run geocodeMissing() once and authorize.

const LOCATIONIQ_KEY = 'YOUR_LOCATIONIQ_KEY_HERE'; // paste your key

function geocodeMissing(){
  const ss = SpreadsheetApp.getActive();
  const sheet = ss.getSheetByName('Contractors');
  const data = sheet.getDataRange().getValues();
  // find columns
  const headers = data[0];
  const zipCol = headers.indexOf('zip') + 1;
  const latCol = headers.indexOf('lat') + 1;
  const lonCol = headers.indexOf('lon') + 1;
  for(let r=1;r<data.length;r++){
    const zip = sheet.getRange(r+1, zipCol).getValue();
    const lat = sheet.getRange(r+1, latCol).getValue();
    const lon = sheet.getRange(r+1, lonCol).getValue();
    if(zip && (!lat || !lon)){
      const url = `https://us1.locationiq.com/v1/search.php?key=${LOCATIONIQ_KEY}&postalcode=${encodeURIComponent(zip)}&country=US&format=json&limit=1`;
      try {
        const resp = UrlFetchApp.fetch(url, {muteHttpExceptions:true});
        const j = JSON.parse(resp.getContentText());
        if(j && j[0]){
          sheet.getRange(r+1, latCol).setValue(j[0].lat);
          sheet.getRange(r+1, lonCol).setValue(j[0].lon);
          Utilities.sleep(1100); // respect rate limits
        }
      } catch(e){ Logger.log('geocode error row '+(r+1)+': '+e) }
    }
  }
}
Run geocodeMissing() once; schedule daily if you want more.

PART G ‚Äî MATCHING: Haversine formula in Sheets (distance calculation)
Add helper columns on Leads or in a new sheet Matches_Calc. You‚Äôll compute distance between each lead and each contractor.

Simpler approach (pre-built formula for one contractor):

match_1_id, match_1_score, match_1_distance, etc.

But the clean approach is to use Apps Script to compute top 3 candidates for each new lead (recommended).

PART H ‚Äî APPS SCRIPT: Match logic + Email notifications (complete ready code)
The script below:

Runs when a new lead row is added (you can set a trigger).

Searches Contractors for service match + within radius.

Computes composite score per contractor.

Picks top 3 and writes to Matches sheet and sends email to those contractors with lead details.

Steps:

In Apps Script (same project), create a new script and paste the code below.

Replace the FROM_EMAIL if needed and ensure the Gmail account can send emails.

Authorize and then set up an onFormSubmit trigger (or a time-driven trigger every 5 minutes).

Apps Script code (copy/paste and update API key placeholders):

// ------- CONFIG -------
const EARTH_RADIUS_MILES = 3958.8;
const FROM_EMAIL = Session.getActiveUser().getEmail(); // your google email
// ------- END CONFIG -------

function haversineMiles(lat1, lon1, lat2, lon2){
  // convert to radians
  const toRad = v => v * Math.PI/180;
  const dLat = toRad(lat2 - lat1);
  const dLon = toRad(lon2 - lon1);
  const a = Math.sin(dLat/2)*Math.sin(dLat/2) + Math.cos(toRad(lat1))*Math.cos(toRad(lat2))*Math.sin(dLon/2)*Math.sin(dLon/2);
  const c = 2 * Math.atan2(Math.sqrt(a), Math.sqrt(1-a));
  return EARTH_RADIUS_MILES * c;
}

function computeMatchScore(lead, contractor){
  // lead: {lat, lon, tags:[], budget_min, budget_max}
  // contractor: {lat, lon, services:[], min,max,capacity,verified,rating}
  // Required: service match else return 0
  let score = 0;
  const intersect = lead.project_tags.split(',').map(t=>t.trim().toLowerCase()).filter(Boolean)
                    .some(t => contractor.service_tags.toLowerCase().split(',').map(x=>x.trim()).indexOf(t) !== -1);
  if(!intersect) return 0;
  score += 40; // service match base

  // distance score
  if(lead.lat && lead.lon && contractor.lat && contractor.lon){
    const d = haversineMiles(Number(lead.lat), Number(lead.lon), Number(contractor.lat), Number(contractor.lon));
    if(d <= 10) score += 30;
    else if(d <= 25) score += 20;
    else if(d <= 50) score += 10;
    // add distance to return object too
  }

  // budget fit
  if(contractor.typical_project_min && contractor.typical_project_max && lead.budget_min && lead.budget_max){
    const overlap = !(contractor.typical_project_max < lead.budget_min || contractor.typical_project_min > lead.budget_max);
    if(overlap) score += 20;
    else score += 10;
  }

  // capacity
  if(Number(contractor.capacity_per_month) > 0) score += 5;

  // verified + rating
  if(contractor.verified && contractor.verified.toString().toLowerCase() === 'yes') score += 3;
  if(contractor.rating && Number(contractor.rating) >= 4.5) score += 2;

  return score;
}

function findTopMatchesForLead(leadRowIndex){
  const ss = SpreadsheetApp.getActive();
  const leadsS = ss.getSheetByName('Leads');
  const contractorsS = ss.getSheetByName('Contractors');
  const matchesS = ss.getSheetByName('Matches');

  const leadRow = leadsS.getRange(leadRowIndex,1,1, leadsS.getLastColumn()).getValues()[0];
  const headersLead = leadsS.getRange(1,1,1,leadsS.getLastColumn()).getValues()[0];
  const leadObj = {};
  headersLead.forEach((h,i)=> leadObj[h]=leadRow[i]);

  // load contractors into array
  const contractors = contractorsS.getDataRange().getValues();
  const headersC = contractors[0];
  const contractorsObjs = [];
  for(let r=1;r<contractors.length;r++){
    const row = contractors[r];
    const obj = {};
    headersC.forEach((h,i)=> obj[h]=row[i]);
    contractorsObjs.push(obj);
  }

  const scored = [];
  contractorsObjs.forEach(c => {
    const s = computeMatchScore(leadObj,c);
    if(s>0) {
      const d = (leadObj.lat && leadObj.lon && c.lat && c.lon) ? haversineMiles(Number(leadObj.lat),Number(leadObj.lon),Number(c.lat),Number(c.lon)) : 9999;
      scored.push({contractor:c,score:s,distance:d});
    }
  });
  scored.sort((a,b)=> b.score - a.score);
  const top = scored.slice(0,3);

  // write matches & send emails
  const now = new Date();
  top.forEach(t => {
    const matchId = 'M' + Utilities.getUuid().slice(0,8);
    matchesS.appendRow([matchId, leadObj.lead_id || ('L' + Utilities.getUuid().slice(0,6)), t.contractor.contractor_id, now, t.distance, t.score, 'Sent', now, '', '']);
    // send email to contractor
    const subject = `New lead: ${leadObj.project_tags} in ${leadObj.zip}`;
    const body = `Hi ${t.contractor.contact_name || t.contractor.business_name},\n\nWe have a new homeowner lead that matches your services:\n\nProject: ${leadObj.project_tags}\nBudget: ${leadObj.budget_range || (leadObj.budget_min + '-' + leadObj.budget_max)}\nLocation: ${leadObj.city || leadObj.zip}\nContact: ${leadObj.first_name} ${leadObj.last_name} ‚Äî ${leadObj.email} ${leadObj.phone}\n\nReply to this email if you‚Äôd like the lead details and we‚Äôll confirm if it‚Äôs still available.\n\nBest,\nSophie ‚Äî Lumo22\n`;
    MailApp.sendEmail(t.contractor.email, subject, body, {from: FROM_EMAIL});
  });

  // update Leads sheet matched_contractor_ids and status
  const matchedIds = top.map(t => t.contractor.contractor_id).join(',');
  const leadRowNum = leadRowIndex;
  const matchedColIndex = headersLead.indexOf('matched_contractor_ids') + 1;
  const statusColIndex = headersLead.indexOf('match_status') + 1;
  if(matchedColIndex>0) leadsS.getRange(leadRowNum, matchedColIndex).setValue(matchedIds);
  if(statusColIndex>0) leadsS.getRange(leadRowNum, statusColIndex).setValue('Matched');
}

// This function can be triggered manually for testing
function runMatchingNow(){
  const ss = SpreadsheetApp.getActive();
  const leadsS = ss.getSheetByName('Leads');
  const lastRow = leadsS.getLastRow();
  // find first New lead (match_status blank or New)
  const data = leadsS.getRange(2,1,lastRow-1, leadsS.getLastColumn()).getValues();
  for(let i=0;i<data.length;i++){
    const row = data[i];
    const headersLead = leadsS.getRange(1,1,1,leadsS.getLastColumn()).getValues()[0];
    const status = row[headersLead.indexOf('match_status')];
    if(!status || status.toString().toLowerCase() === 'new'){
      // call matching on this row (row index = i+2)
      findTopMatchesForLead(i+2);
      break;
    }
  }
}
How to wire the script to run automatically:

In Apps Script editor: Triggers (left) ‚Üí Add Trigger

Function: runMatchingNow

Event: Time-driven ‚Üí Every 5 minutes (or Hourly for cheaper).

Or: if you use Typeform ‚Üí set an onFormSubmit trigger that calls findTopMatchesForLead with the row number. Simpler: run runMatchingNow every 5 min to pick any new lead.

Notes & Safety:

Gmail sending limits apply (~100‚Äì1500/day depending on account type). Start small.

Test thoroughly with your own email addresses first.

‚öôÔ∏è HOW MATCHING WILL WORK (USA version)

a Python script (if scraping manually), or

an automation in Make/Zapier using ZIP-to-lat/lon lookup (free databases exist).

But here‚Äôs the clean way to think about it:

‚úÖ Should the contractor form ALSO have a disclaimer?
Short answer: Yes ‚Äî but a shorter, lighter one.

Why?

Because:

Contractors need to confirm they understand they're not employees, and you‚Äôre not guaranteeing them work (protects YOU).

Clients need to confirm they understand you‚Äôre not responsible for managing, supervising, or guaranteeing the performance of contractors (protects YOU).

Both sides require different disclaimers with different legal purposes.

üü¶ Client Form Disclaimer (STRONGER) ‚Äî the one you requested
Here is the U.S.-appropriate version to replace the old Q12 meaning:

Client Disclaimer & Consent (FINAL STEP)
By submitting this form, you acknowledge and agree that:

Lumo22 is not responsible for the performance, work quality, timelines, or conduct of any contractor recommended or introduced to you.

Lumo22 does not guarantee contractor availability, suitability, or outcomes.

It is the client‚Äôs responsibility to conduct their own due diligence, negotiate terms, and enter into a direct agreement with any contractor.

Lumo22 does not act as an agency, employer, or management company, and makes no promises to secure talent or deliver services.

Lumo22 is not liable for any loss, damages, or disputes arising from contractor engagement.

Do you agree to these terms?
‚Üí Yes / No

This belongs ONLY on the client form.

üü© Contractor Form Disclaimer (OPTIONAL BUT RECOMMENDED ‚Äî SHORT)

PART C ‚Äî Bright Data: create your first scraper (Craigslist example)

Goal: Scrape the ‚Äúgigs‚Äù and ‚Äúservices wanted‚Äù categories for target keywords and push to Airtable.

1. Sign in to Bright Data ‚Üí Web Scraper IDE
Create new project ‚Üí name: Lumo22_Craigslist_JobLeads

2. Create starting URLs
You‚Äôll need a list of city pages to target. Start with 3 seed cities (pick two to begin). Example:

https://austin.craigslist.org/d/services/search/biz
https://miami.craigslist.org/d/services/search/biz
https://losangeles.craigslist.org/d/services/search/biz
(For gigs: use /d/gigs/search/gg or search/biz depending on category ‚Äî pick the one with homeowner requests.)

3. Build the crawler workflow (visual steps in Bright Data)
Step A: open the listing page (starting URL).

posting_title ‚Üí the link text

posting_url ‚Üí href

posting_date ‚Üí date snippet if available

location ‚Üí location snippet (if present)

posting_title (H1)

posting_body (full description)

posting_images (list of image srcs)

contact_email (if present in body)

contact_phone (if in body)

posting_location (if present)

posting_date (detailed)

Step D: store the result object.

4. Filters / keyword matching (important)
Only keep postings that contain your keywords. Add a filter in Bright Data to only return postings where the posting_body OR posting_title (case-insensitive) contains any of these keywords:

"landscap", "deck", "patio", "pergola", "fence", "driveway", "hardscape", "outdoor kitchen", "firepit", "lighting", "turf", "pool"
(Using stems like ‚Äúlandscap‚Äù catches ‚Äúlandscaping‚Äù, ‚Äúlandscaper‚Äù etc.)

5. Output schema mapping
Map Bright Data fields to Airtable fields:

posting_title ‚Üí Project Title

posting_body ‚Üí Project Description

Keyword match ‚Üí Category (you can programmatically set: if "deck" in text ‚Üí Category = Patio / Decking; else if "landscap" ‚Üí Landscaping & Garden Design; else Other)

posting_location ‚Üí Location

Extract ZIP if available ‚Üí ZIP

contact_email ‚Üí Contact Email

contact_phone ‚Üí Contact Phone

posting_images ‚Üí Images Available (checkbox = true if non-empty)

posting_url ‚Üí Source URL

posting_date ‚Üí Date Scraped (set to current run timestamp)

Bright Data allows transforming outputs ‚Äî set Category by simple conditional mapping (many scrapers offer a transform rule). If not supported in UI, send raw posting and set Category in Make.com.

6. Scheduling
Set the scraper Run frequency to every 6 hours (or every 12 hours when small).

7. Test run
Run the scraper once and inspect output. Save the JSON/CSV.

PART D ‚Äî Make.com: push scraper output to Airtable

posting_body ‚Üí Project Description

contact_name ‚Üí Contact Name

contact_phone ‚Üí Contact Phone

now ‚Üí Date Scraped

Status ‚Üí New

Test the scenario with one run and confirm records appear in Airtable correctly.

PART E ‚Äî Category derivation logic (simple in Make)
If Bright Data does not give you structured category, do the following in Make (text parser):

For each posting, convert posting_title + posting_body to lowercase.

If contains "deck" or "patio" ‚Üí Category = Patio / Decking

Else if contains "landscap" or "gardens" ‚Üí Landscaping & Garden Design

Else if contains "pergola", "gazebo", "pergolas" ‚Üí Pergolas / Gazebos / Outdoor Structures

Else if contains "driveway", "paving" ‚Üí Driveways & Hardscaping

Else if contains "fence" ‚Üí Fencing & Gates

Else if contains "turf", "artificial" ‚Üí Artificial Turf Installation

Else if contains "lighting", "irrigation" ‚Üí Outdoor Lighting & Irrigation

Else if contains "pool" or "spa" ‚Üí Pool & Spa Projects

Else ‚Üí Other

Implement those as sequential text-match checks in Make; set Category to the first match.

PART F ‚Äî Deduplication & validation rules (critical)
Add a Make step before creating an Airtable record:

Search Airtable by Source URL ‚Äî if exists ‚Üí skip (avoid duplicates).

Else search by posting_title + ZIP ‚Äî if similar existing record found (fuzzy match), skip.

If a posting has no contact info, mark Status = Needs Review so a human can check.

When it asks ‚ÄúSelect all similar‚Äù accept to capture all previews.

In the right panel add these fields for the preview item:

posting_title ‚Üí select the link text. (Type: Text)

posting_url ‚Üí select the link href. (Type: URL)

posting_date_preview ‚Üí select date snippet if present. (Type: Text/Date)

Add a Click / follow action on that preview element to open the detail page for each post. Label it OpenDetail.

Step 4 ‚Äî On the detail page, extract required fields
After the click action, the scraper opens each posting detail page. Now use the selector to pick elements and add fields:

posting_title ‚Üí H1 (text)

posting_body ‚Üí main body/description (long text)

posting_images ‚Üí select image elements (collect srcs) ‚Äî set as array

posting_contact_email ‚Üí search the body for mailto: (if IDE supports extraction) or extract any visible email-like text (text).

posting_contact_phone ‚Üí extract any phone-number-like text if visible.

posting_location ‚Üí location snippet (text)

posting_date ‚Üí date if present

posting_url ‚Üí page URL (auto)

For each field, give the exact field name (use names above) and appropriate type (Text / Array / URL / Date).

Step 5 ‚Äî Add pagination (so it scrapes more listings)
Back on the listing page in record mode, locate the Next button (pagination).

Click it and choose Follow / Click for pagination.

Configure the scraper to follow pagination up to a max pages limit (start with 5 pages). This is often in the pagination settings.

Step 6 ‚Äî Add keyword filter (reduce noise)
You only want posts containing target keywords. Add a conditional filter after scraping the detail page:

Create a filter step (some IDEs call it ‚ÄúFilter results‚Äù or ‚ÄúPost-processing‚Äù).

Use a case-insensitive text contains check on posting_title + posting_body.

Keywords to use (copy/paste):

landscap, deck, patio, pergola, fence, driveway, hardscape, outdoor kitchen, firepit, lighting, turf, pool
Filter rule pseudo-expression (Bright Data UI will have forms; adapt):

if lower(posting_title || " " || posting_body) contains any of ["landscap","deck","patio","pergola","fence","driveway","hardscape","outdoor kitchen","firepit","lighting","turf","pool"] then keep else discard
This reduces irrelevant posts.

Step 7 ‚Äî Output mapping & format
In the Output / Storage section of the scraper, choose JSON or Webhook output. We‚Äôll later fetch results from Bright Data via Make.

Best option for Make: Enable Webhook / API output (if Bright Data supports it) so Make can retrieve results. If not:

Choose JSON output and Save runs ‚Äî you can pull the JSON from Bright Data‚Äôs run/API endpoint.

Save the output schema mapping so fields match Airtable:

posting_title ‚Üí Project Title

posting_body ‚Üí Project Description

derived_category ‚Üí Category (we'll set category in Make)

posting_location ‚Üí Location

posting_city ‚Üí City (if you can parse)

posting_state ‚Üí State (parse)

posting_zip ‚Üí ZIP (parse if available)

posting_images array length ‚Üí Images Available (boolean)

posting_url ‚Üí Source URL

posting_contact_email ‚Üí Contact Email

posting_contact_phone ‚Üí Contact Phone

posting_date ‚Üí Date Scraped

(If Bright Data cannot parse city/state/postcode reliably, send raw location string and parse in Make.)

Step 8 ‚Äî Scheduling
In Scraper settings, find Run schedule or Scheduling.

Set a schedule to Run every 6 hours. (If budget sensitive, start every 12 hours.)

Turn on notifications on failures.

Step 9 ‚Äî Test run & inspect results
Click Run (or Run now). Wait for the run to finish.

Open Run results ‚Üí inspect several items. Check:

Titles captured?

Body text?

Contact email/phone extracted?

Source URL correct?

Filter worked (irrelevant posts removed)?

If many irrelevant results, tighten keyword list or increase detail filtering.

Step 10 ‚Äî Connect Bright Data output to Make.com (two ways)
Option A ‚Äî Webhook (preferred)
In Bright Data output choose Webhook and paste a Make webhook URL:

In Make.com create a new Scenario ‚Üí Add Webhook (Custom webhook) module ‚Üí Copy the webhook URL.

In Bright Data, set Webhook URL = Make webhook URL.

Bright Data will POST JSON per run to Make.

In Make, add an Iterator after the webhook and map fields to Airtable Create record module.

Option B ‚Äî Pull JSON from Bright Data API
Bright Data stores run results with an API endpoint:

In Make use an HTTP > Get module to fetch Bright Data run JSON endpoint.

Iterate results and map to Airtable.

I recommend Option A (Webhook) ‚Äî simpler and near-real-time.

Step 11 ‚Äî Make.com mapping basics (quick)
When you map fields in Make ‚Üí Airtable, map exactly:

posting_title ‚Üí Project Title

posting_body ‚Üí Project Description

category (derived in Make based on keywords) ‚Üí Category

posting_location ‚Üí Location

parsed city ‚Üí City

parsed state ‚Üí State

parsed zip ‚Üí ZIP

posting_images array length > 0 ‚Üí Images Available (true/false)

posting_url ‚Üí Source URL

posting_contact_email ‚Üí Contact Email

posting_contact_phone ‚Üí Contact Phone

now() ‚Üí Date Scraped

Status ‚Üí New

Add a search Airtable record module before "Create record" to dedupe by Source URL ‚Äî if found, skip.

Step 12 ‚Äî Tuning & production testing
Run the scraper for 48 hours. Review 50‚Äì100 records.

Adjust keyword filters and category derivation rules in Make if needed.

When confident, add other sources by creating additional scrapers with similar selectors (Nextdoor public, Thumbtack public requests, public Facebook Marketplace posts).

Troubleshooting tips
If Bright Data returns many blank posting_contact_email, accept that many posts hide contact details ‚Äî mark those records Needs Review in Airtable.

If scraper is blocked, slow down crawl rate or enable Bright Data proxy rotation.

If pagination returns duplicates, enable dedupe post-processing or include posting_url check.

ChatGPT said:
Short answer: No ‚Äî you do not need a VPN to the USA to use Bright Data.

Here‚Äôs why:

‚úÖ Bright Data already provides U.S. proxies
Bright Data is built specifically for global web scraping.
When you run a scraper:

It does not use your home IP, and

It does not care where you physically are

Instead, each scraper automatically uses Bright Data‚Äôs proxy network, choosing U.S. IPs when scraping U.S. sites.

So even if you're in the UK:

‚úî You can scrape U.S. websites
‚úî You will appear as a U.S. user
‚úî You will not be blocked for being in the UK
‚úî You don‚Äôt need a VPN

‚ùó When WOULD you need a VPN?
Only in these rare cases:

1. If a U.S.-only website blocks sign-ups from UK IPs
(e.g., a platform requiring a U.S. phone number to register).

Not relevant for Bright Data scrapers.

2. If you were manually browsing those websites in your own browser
(i.e., not scraping).

Still not required for Bright Data.

üü© Conclusion
You can continue:

No VPN. No location change needed.
Bright Data handles the U.S. location for you automatically.

The Web Scraper IDE is Bright Data‚Äôs fully hosted JavaScript development environment for building custom web scrapers. It‚Äôs designed for developers who want granular control over their data collection, without worrying about infrastructure, proxies, or anti-bot measures.

Here‚Äôs how you can use the Web Scraper IDE:

Access the IDE:

Go to the ‚ÄúWeb Data Collection‚Äù section.
Navigate to the ‚ÄúMy Scrapers‚Äù tab.
Click ‚ÄúDevelop a Web Scraper (IDE)‚Äù to start building your scraper.
Start from scratch or use a template:

You can begin with a blank project or select a pre-built template for popular websites.
Write your interaction code:

Use JavaScript to interact with elements on the target website (e.g., clicking buttons, filling forms).
Write your parser code:

Parse the HTML or data you‚Äôve collected to extract the information you need.
Preview and test:

Use the play icon to run your code and preview the results.
Save your scraper:

Once satisfied, save your code.
Set delivery preferences:

Choose how you want to receive your data (e.g., JSON, CSV, XLSX). More info: Delivery settings
Initiate the scraper run:

Start your data collection and retrieve results. More info: Initiate your scraper
You can also use the AI code generator in the IDE by entering a target URL, and it will generate a ready-to-use code template for you.

For a step-by-step guide, visit: Develop a self-managed scraper

Inside the IDE you will see 2 tabs:

Interaction code

Parser code

Replace them exactly with the following.

‚úèÔ∏è INTERACTION CODE (copy/paste this EXACTLY)
async function main(ctx) {
  const page = await ctx.newPage();

  // Load Craigslist services/gigs page
  await page.goto(ctx.data.input_url, { waitUntil: 'networkidle2' });

  // Wait for results to load
  await page.waitForSelector('.result-row', { timeout: 15000 });

  // Return the HTML for parsing
  return {
    html: await page.content(),
    url: ctx.data.input_url
  };
}
‚úèÔ∏è PARSER CODE (copy/paste this EXACTLY)
function parse(ctx) {
  const $ = ctx.cheerio;

  const results = [];

  $('.result-row').each((i, el) => {
    const title = $(el).find('.result-title').text().trim();
    const link = $(el).find('.result-title').attr('href');
    const date = $(el).find('time').attr('datetime');
    const price = $(el).find('.result-price').text().trim() || null;
    const hood = $(el).find('.result-hood').text().trim().replace(/[()]/g, '') || null;

    results.push({
      title,
      link,
      date,
      price,
      neighborhood: hood
    });
  });

  return results;
}
‚úÖ STEP 4 ‚Äî Add the Input Field
Click the ‚ÄúSettings‚Äù tab inside the IDE.

Scroll to ‚ÄúInput schema‚Äù

Add ONE field:

input_url  (type: string)
This lets you change the Craigslist city later.

‚úÖ Step-by-step: Start adding your first ‚Äústage‚Äù
We will scrape the simplest website first so you can get results quickly.
Let‚Äôs start with homeguide.com because it‚Äôs predictable and easy to parse.

1. Click ‚ÄúNew stage‚Äù
This creates a stage inside Interaction Code.

Name it:

homeguide_main_page
2. Paste this into Interaction Code (copy/paste):
async function main(page, context) {

  // Load the main category page for landscapers
  await page.goto("https://homeguide.com/c/landscaping");

  // Allow the page to fully load
  await page.waitForTimeout(3000);

  // Extract all business listing links
  const links = await page.$$eval("a", (anchors) =>
    anchors
      .map(a => a.href)
      .filter(href => href.includes("/pros/"))
  );

  // Save each link for parsing in the next stage
  for (const link of links) {
    await context.enqueueRequest({ url: link });
  }

}
‚úî This tells the scraper to visit homeguide.com ‚Üí landscaping
‚úî Collect all the business profile links
‚úî Send each link to the Parser code
‚úî Parser code will extract name, phone, email, etc.

3. Click ‚ÄúParser code‚Äù tab
Paste this:

async function parse(context) {

  const name = await context.page.$eval("h1", el => el.textContent.trim()).catch(() => null);
  const phone = await context.page.$eval("a[href^='tel:']", el => el.textContent.trim()).catch(() => null);

  return {
    url: context.request.url,
    name,
    phone,
    source: "homeguide",
    date_scraped: new Date().toISOString()
  };
}
4. Click ‚ÄúSave‚Äù (top right)
5. Test Run
Click "Initiate manually" ‚Üí Select:

Run type: Single run

Depth: 1

Press Start

You should get:

a list of landscaper contractors

with name + phone

delivered inside Bright Data

exportable to Airtable / Make / Sheets

Here‚Äôs why you can‚Äôt see Save and exactly what to do next.

‚úÖ Why the Save button is missing in the Web Scraper IDE
In Bright Data, you can only save a scraper after you create at least ONE ‚Äústage‚Äù.

A stage = a block of code (Interaction or Parser) that the scraper will run.

If the scraper has no stages, the Save button stays hidden/disabled.

‚úÖ How to make the Save button appear
1. Click ‚ÄúNew stage‚Äù (right-hand side)
You must add one stage before Save becomes available.

When you click New Stage, it will ask:

‚ÄúInteraction stage‚Äù
or

‚ÄúParser stage‚Äù

Choose: Interaction stage (the first one).

2. Give the stage a name
For example:

‚ÄúLoad page‚Äù

‚ÄúScrape listings‚Äù

‚ÄúStep1‚Äù

You can name it anything.

‚ö†Ô∏è If the stage name box is empty ‚Üí Save will NOT appear.

3. Add any code to the Interaction Stage
Even one line works:

async function interaction(page) {
  await page.goto("https://www.remax.eu/en/properties");
}
(You can copy/paste that exactly ‚Äî it‚Äôs only to unlock the Save button.)

‚ö†Ô∏è If the code box is empty ‚Üí Save will NOT appear.

After you do all three things
‚úî Add a stage
‚úî Name it
‚úî Put at least one line of code in the stage

üëâ The Save button appears at the top-right.

or

Depending on your screen size.

1) Create the new scraper project
Left menu ‚Üí Data ‚Üí Scrapers ‚Üí My Scrapers.

Click + New Scraper or Create Scraper.

Choose Start from scratch (not AI template).

Name the project: Lumo22_Craigslist_JobLeads (you can edit later).

Click Create ‚Äî the IDE opens.

You should now see the IDE with panels: Interaction code, Parser code, New stage, Finish editing (Save).

2) Add the first Interaction stage (listings page)
Click New stage ‚Üí select Interaction stage.

Name the stage: listings_stage.

In the Interaction code editor, paste this exact code:

// LISTINGS stage ‚Äî load the city listings page and enqueue detail pages
async function interaction(ctx) {
  const page = await ctx.newPage();
  const url = ctx.data.input_url; // provided at run-time

  await page.goto(url, { waitUntil: 'networkidle2' });
  await page.waitForTimeout(2000);

  // collect listing links on this page
  const links = await page.$$eval('.result-row .result-title', els =>
    els.map(el => el.href || el.getAttribute('href')).filter(Boolean)
  );

  // enqueue each listing URL for parsing
  for (const link of links) {
    await ctx.enqueueRequest({ url: link, userData: { source: url } });
  }

  // attempt to click "next" if present (for pagination)
  const next = await page.$('a.button.next');
  if (next) {
    const nextUrl = await page.$eval('a.button.next', a => a.href);
    if (nextUrl) {
      await ctx.enqueueRequest({ url: nextUrl, userData: { source: url, isPagination:true } });
    }
  }

  await page.close();
}
Click Finish editing (this saves the stage).

3) Add the Parser stage (detail pages)
Click New stage ‚Üí choose Parser stage.

Name it: detail_stage.

Switch to Parser code tab and paste this exact code:

// DETAIL stage ‚Äî parse full listing page
async function parse(ctx) {
  const $ = ctx.cheerio;

  const title = $('h2.postingtitletext').text().trim() ||
                $('h1').first().text().trim();
  const body = $('#postingbody').text().trim() ||
               $('section').text().trim();
  // numeric phone/email extraction via regex fallbacks
  const bodyText = body || '';
  const emailMatch = bodyText.match(/[A-Z0-9._%+-]+@[A-Z0-9.-]+\.[A-Z]{2,}/i);
  const phoneMatch = bodyText.match(/(\+?\d[\d\-\s\(\)]{7,}\d)/);

  // try to extract location & price
  const hood = $('.postingtitletext .result-hood').text().replace(/[()]/g,'').trim() || null;
  const price = $('.price').first().text().trim() || null;

  return {
    source_url: ctx.request.url,
    title: title || null,
    description: bodyText || null,
    neighborhood: hood,
    price: price,
    contact_email: emailMatch ? emailMatch[0] : null,
    contact_phone: phoneMatch ? phoneMatch[0].replace(/\s+/g,' ').trim() : null,
    date_scraped: new Date().toISOString(),
    scraped_from: ctx.userData && ctx.userData.source ? ctx.userData.source : null
  };
}
Click Finish editing (save parser stage).

4) Add input schema so you can run per-city
In the IDE top area click Settings (if present) or find Input schema in the project settings panel.

Add one input variable:

Name: input_url

Type: string

Example: https://newyork.craigslist.org/d/gigs/search/ggg

Click Finish editing (save settings / project).

5) Test-run for a single city
From the IDE top bar click Initiate manually (now enabled).

Choose Single run or Run with input ‚Üí paste the test URL:

https://newyork.craigslist.org/d/gigs/search/ggg
Start run ‚Üí wait for it to complete.

Open Run Results / Logs ‚Üí inspect items. You should see parsed JSON objects with title, description, contact_email, contact_phone, source_url, date_scraped.

If the page structure differs, tweak selectors (e.g., .result-row vs .result-title) ‚Äî but usually the provided selectors work for standard Craigslist layout.

6) Send results to Make (webhook) ‚Üí Airtable
We‚Äôll set up Make to receive scraped records in real time.

A ‚Äî Create a Make webhook
Login to Make.com ‚Üí create New Scenario.

Add the module Webhooks ‚Üí Custom webhook ‚Üí Add a webhook. Name it BrightData_Craigslist_Webhook. Copy the Webhook URL Make gives you.

B ‚Äî Add webhook as Bright Data delivery
Back in Bright Data IDE (project page) ‚Üí find Delivery settings or Outputs.

Add new delivery destination ‚Üí choose Webhook (POST).

Paste the Make webhook URL. Configure content type: JSON. Save.

(If Bright Data requires a secret header, you can add one and then configure Make to check it. Not required for quick start.)

C ‚Äî Add an iterator & Airtable create records in Make
In your Make scenario: after the Webhook module add Iterator (if the webhook posts an array).

Add Airtable ‚Üí Create record (connect your Airtable account). Map fields:

Make field	Airtable field (Project Leads)
title	Project Title
description	Project Description
neighborhood / location	Location / City
price	Budget
contact_email	Contact Email
contact_phone	Contact Phone
source_url	Source URL
date_scraped	Date Scraped
scraped_from	Notes or Source
category (see step 7)	Category (set in Make by keyword mapping)
Status	New
Save the scenario and run it once (or set it to ‚ÄúOn‚Äù).

D ‚Äî Test end-to-end
In Bright Data run the scraper again.

Confirm entries appear in Airtable table Project Leads (Scraped).

7) Category derivation (automatic)
In Make, before creating Airtable record add a Router / Text aggregator module to set Category based on keywords in title + description. Use this logic (copy/paste pseudo logic into Make text filters):

If contains "deck" or "patio" ‚Üí Decking & Patios

If contains "landscap" or "garden" ‚Üí Landscaping

If contains "pergola" or "gazebo" ‚Üí Pergolas / Outdoor Structures

If contains "driveway" or "paving" ‚Üí Driveways & Hardscaping

If contains "fence" ‚Üí Fencing

If contains "turf" or "artificial" ‚Üí Artificial Turf Installation

If contains "kitchen" or "outdoor kitchen" or "firepit" ‚Üí Outdoor Kitchens & Firepits

If contains "pool" or "spa" ‚Üí Pool & Spa Projects

Else ‚Üí Other

Implement as sequential if/else in Make.

8) Dedupe rules (in Make, before Create record)
Use Search records Airtable module to find existing records with the same source_url.

If found ‚Üí Update status or skip.

If not found ‚Üí Create new record.

Also consider fuzzy check by title + ZIP (optional).

9) Scheduling & throttling
In Bright Data set the scraper Schedule: every 6‚Äì12 hours for each city. Start 12 hours to conserve credits.

Use low concurrency (1‚Äì3) and a 2000ms wait between requests to reduce blocks. Bright Data default proxy pool will help.

10) Expand to more cities
Maintain a spreadsheet of city start URLs, e.g.:
https://{city}.craigslist.org/d/gigs/search/ggg
Replace {city} with losangeles, chicago, dallas, phoenix, miami, etc.

Either run individual scraper runs per city (paste url into input) or set up a scheduler / batch run that loops through a list of inputs.

11) Monitor & tune
Weekly: spot-check 20 records for quality and correct category.

Tighten keyword filters if noise increases.

Move poor-quality sources to ‚ÄúNeeds Review‚Äù until you have reliable ones.

Quick testing checklist (do this now)

Confirm Bright Data returns parsed results.

Set up Make webhook and map fields to Airtable.

Run scraper again and confirm Airtable rows appear.

Verify dedupe logic (run same listing twice ‚Üí Airtable should skip duplicate).

Tune category mapping in Make until accuracy > 70%.

In Interaction code replace any prefilled content with this exact code (copy/paste):

// LISTINGS stage ‚Äî load the city listings page and enqueue detail pages
async function interaction(ctx) {
  const page = await ctx.newPage();
  const url = ctx.data.input_url || input_url || ''; // support both ctx.data and legacy

  if (!url) {
    throw new Error('input_url is required');
  }

  await page.goto(url, { waitUntil: 'networkidle2' });
  await page.waitForTimeout(1800);

  // collect listing links on this page
  const links = await page.$$eval('.result-row .result-title', els =>
    els.map(el => el.href || el.getAttribute('href')).filter(Boolean)
  );

  // enqueue each listing URL for parsing
  for (const link of links) {
    await ctx.enqueueRequest({ url: link, userData: { source: url } });
  }

  // attempt to get "next" pagination link and enqueue it
  try {
    const nextUrl = await page.$eval('a.button.next', a => a.href).catch(() => null);
    if (nextUrl) {
      await ctx.enqueueRequest({ url: nextUrl, userData: { source: url, isPagination: true } });
    }
  } catch(e){
    // ignore if no next
  }

  await page.close();
}
Click Finish editing (this saves the stage).

STEP 4 ‚Äî Add the DETAIL Parser stage
Click New stage ‚Üí Parser stage.

Name it: detail_stage.

Switch to Parser code and paste this exact code:

// DETAIL stage ‚Äî parse full listing page
async function parse(ctx) {
  const $ = ctx.cheerio;

  // Title heuristics
  const title = $('h2.postingtitletext').text().trim() || $('h1').first().text().trim() || $('title').text().trim();

  // Body text
  const body = ($('#postingbody').text() || $('section').text() || '').trim();
  const bodyText = body;

  // Regex: email and phone
  const emailMatch = bodyText.match(/[A-Z0-9._%+-]+@[A-Z0-9.-]+\.[A-Z]{2,}/i);
  const phoneMatch = bodyText.match(/(\+?\d[\d\-\s\(\)]{7,}\d)/);

  // location & price
  const hood = ($('.postingtitletext .result-hood').text().replace(/[()]/g,'') || '').trim() || null;
  const price = ($('.price').first().text() || '').trim() || null;

  return {
    source_url: ctx.request.url,
    title: title || null,
    description: bodyText || null,
    neighborhood: hood,
    price: price,
    contact_email: emailMatch ? emailMatch[0] : null,
    contact_phone: phoneMatch ? phoneMatch[0].replace(/\s+/g,' ').trim() : null,
    date_scraped: new Date().toISOString(),
    scraped_from: ctx.userData?.source || null
  };
}
Click Finish editing (save parser stage).

STEP 5 ‚Äî Verify stages & Save
Ensure both stages appear under Stages: listings_stage and detail_stage.

Confirm Inputs show input_url.

Click Finish editing or the top-right Save (it may say Finish editing depending on UI).

STEP 6 ‚Äî Run a test (single city)
Click Initiate manually (top bar) ‚Üí choose Single run.

For input_url paste:
https://newyork.craigslist.org/d/gigs/search/ggg

Click Start / Run.

Open Run Results / Logs when finished. You should see parsed JSON objects like {title, description, contact_email, contact_phone, source_url‚Ä¶}.

If you get errors, copy/paste the error message here. Common fixes: adjust selectors (Craigslist HTML varies slightly), increase waitForTimeout, or change .result-row .result-title selector to match page structure (you can inspect the page to confirm).

Open Make.com ‚Üí New Scenario ‚Üí add module Webhooks ‚Üí Custom webhook ‚Üí Add a webhook. Name it BrightData_Craigslist_Webhook.

Copy the Webhook URL Make gives you.

B ‚Äî Configure Bright Data delivery to webhook
In Bright Data IDE view, find Delivery / Output / Delivery settings (may be in left panel or settings).

Add Webhook destination: Paste the Make webhook URL. Set content type = JSON. Save.

If Bright Data asks for a per-record delivery format, use JSON array per run or POST each item (the webhook will accept either).

C ‚Äî Build Make scenario: webhook ‚Üí iterator ‚Üí Airtable
In Make, after the webhook module add Iterator (if payload is array).

Add Airtable ‚Üí Create Record mapped to Project Leads (Scraped) table in your base. Map fields:

Bright Data field	Airtable field
title	Project Title
description	Project Description
neighborhood	Location
price	Budget
contact_email	Contact Email
contact_phone	Contact Phone
source_url	Source URL
date_scraped	Date Scraped
scraped_from	Notes
category (derive in Make)	Category
Status	New

Map the output to Airtable Category.

Parser returns nulls ‚Üí increase waitForTimeout in interaction stage.

Webhook not receiving ‚Üí confirm Make webhook URL and Bright Data delivery POST settings.

‚úÖ STEP 2 ‚Äî CREATE THE ‚ÄúLISTINGS‚Äù STAGE
1. Click New Stage

(all lowercase, no spaces)

3. Paste this exact code into the Interaction box:
// LISTINGS stage ‚Äî fetch all listing URLs from a Craigslist city section

async function handler(ctx) {
  // Validate input
  if (!input.start_url || !input.city) {
    throw new Error("start_url and city are required");
  }

  await ctx.navigate(input.start_url);

  const $ = ctx.cheerio;

  // Collect listing links
  const links = [];
  $('.result-info a').each((i, el) => {
    const url = $(el).attr('href');
    if (url && url.startsWith('https')) {
      links.push(url);
    }
  });

  // Send each listing to the DETAIL stage
  for (const url of links) {
    await ctx.sendToStage('detail', {
      url: url,
      userData: { source: input.city }
    });
  }

  return { found_listings: links.length };
}
4. Click Finish editing (your Save)
‚úîÔ∏è If it saves with no errors, tell me: ‚ÄúList stage saved.‚Äù
If there is an error, copy/paste it here and I‚Äôll fix it.

‚úÖ STEP 2 ‚Äî Add code to LISTINGS ‚Üí Interaction Code
Click:

Listings ‚Üí Interaction code

DELETE anything that is already in the box.
Then paste this exact code:

async function run(input, ctx) {
  // Load the first page
  await ctx.navigate(input.search_url);

  // Loop through pages
  for (let i = 1; i <= input.max_pages; i++) {
    // Get all listing links on the page
    const links = await ctx.collectLinks("a.result-title");

    for (const link of links) {
      await ctx.addRequest({
        url: link,
        userData: {
          stage: "detail",
          source: input.search_url
        }
      });
    }

    // Click Next button if available
    const hasNext = await ctx.querySelector("a.button.next");
    if (!hasNext) break;

    await ctx.click("a.button.next");
    await ctx.waitForTimeout(1500);
  }
}
‚úîÔ∏è What this does
Opens the craigslist category page

Collects all listing URLs

Sends them to the Detail stage

Clicks ‚ÄúNext‚Äù up to your max pages

‚úÖ STEP 3 ‚Äî Add code to LISTINGS ‚Üí Parser Code
Click:

Listings ‚Üí Parser code

Delete anything inside, then paste this:

async function parse() {
  return {
    status: "listings stage completed"
  };
}
This stage does not need to output data ‚Äî it only sends URLs forward.

‚úÖ STEP 4 ‚Äî Add code to DETAIL ‚Üí Parser Code
Click:

Detail ‚Üí Parser code

DELETE whatever is there, then paste this exact code:

async function parse(ctx) {
  const $ = ctx.cheerio;

  const title = $("h1").text().trim() ||
                $("h2.postingtitletext").text().trim();

  const body = $("#postingbody").text().trim() ||
               $("section").text().trim();

  const bodyText = body || "";

  const emailMatch = bodyText.match(/[A-Z0-9._%+-]+@[A-Z0-9.-]+\.[A-Z]{2,}/i);
  const phoneMatch = bodyText.match(/(\+?\d[\d\-\s\(\)]{7,}\d)/);

  const hood = $(".postingtitletext .result-hood")
    .text()
    .replace(/[()]/g, "")
    .trim() || null;

  const price = $(".price").first().text().trim() || null;

  return {
    source_url: ctx.request.url,
    title: title || null,
    description: bodyText || null,
    neighborhood: hood,
    price: price,
    contact_email: emailMatch ? emailMatch[0] : null,
    contact_phone: phoneMatch ? phoneMatch[0].replace(/\s+/g, " ").trim() : null,
    date_scraped: new Date().toISOString(),
    scraped_from: ctx.userData.source || null
  };
}
